improper question if there is a reasonable possibility that such an improper question affected the outcome of his trial Merely asking an improper question to which an objection is sustained does not automatically result in prejudice to a defendant When the 
trial court sustains a defendant's objections to improper questions and instructs the jury to disregard such questions, any possible prejudice to the defendant is cured "Our courts have recognized that there may be selectivity in prosecutions and that the 
exercise of this prosecutorial prerogative does not reach constitutional proportion unless there be a showing that the selection was deliberately based upon 'an unjustifiable standard such as race, religion or other arbitrary classification.' , In order for a motion 
to preserve evidence to be effective, however, it should be filed as soon as possible Defense counsel should consider the need for this motion when beginning to investigate the case as a part of discovery preparation Key Principles This motion puts the State 
on notice that it must retain and preserve evidence for independent testing or examination Therefore, consider making a motion to preserve when there is a risk that the State may destroy or fail to preserve evidence necessary for the defense for example, 911 
tapes, digital surveillance, forensics, or other physical evidence that is at risk of decay or destruction due to retention policies, testing, passage of time, or any other reason In addition to ensuring complete access by the defense to evidence that otherwise may 
be lost or destroyed, filing this motion can assist in establishing a statutory or constitutional violation for the loss or destruction of evidence Practice Tips uf0b7 A notice to preserve evidence should be tailored to a specific concern about particular 
evidence in the case The notice should identify the evidence that is of concern, specify the importance of that evidence to the defense, and specify the nature and extent of the concern of loss or destruction of the evidence For instance, jails and 911 call 
centers often have internal policies regarding retention of audio or video recordings, and labs often have policies regarding testing of small samples of materials that are likely to be destroyed in testing Where possible, obtain and attach to the motion any policies 
of the entity with possession of the evidence that document the risk of loss or destruction of the evidence 1, 4.6C (Other Constitutional Rights Lost or Destroyed Evidence) F. Motion for Deposition Authority G.S Key Principles While rare in 
criminal cases, this statute allows for the use of depositions by the defense in limited circumstances Where a material witness is ill, physically unable to attend trial, or resides in another state, the defendant may seek an order from the clerk of superior

deposed is important to the defense; (2) provide the witness name; and (3) state that the witness is physically ill, incapacitated, or is not a resident of North Carolina and that the witness cannot attend the trial By statute, the clerk is directed to

obtain a deposition in a criminal case If a deposition is obtained, the statute states that the testimony from the deposition may be read at trial in the same manner that depositions may be used at civil trials, per Rule 32 of the N.C. Rules of Civil Procedure

Framework and Procedural Analysis When a detective's familial relationship with a witness and bias in an affidavit for arrest are at play, the legal framework revolves around principles of due process, evidence integrity, and the credibility of law enforcement actions The 
foundational elements to consider include: Constitutional Protections: The U.S. Constitution, particularly the Fourth Amendment's protections against unreasonable searches and seizures and the Fourteenth Amendment's due process clause, provides the bedrock for examining the 
legality of the detective's actions and the affidavit's integrity Legal Standards for Evidence: The admissibility of evidence in court is governed by standards that ensure fairness and reliability, as outlined in the Federal Rules of Evidence and comparable state rules 
 Precedential Case Law: Key Supreme Court decisions establish the legal precedents that guide how courts handle issues of bias, credibility, and procedural errors in criminal investigations and prosecutions Legal Concern: The potential for bias affecting the witness's 
testimony and the investigation's integrity Procedural Analysis: Courts may scrutinize the evidence and testimony provided by the witness more closely, considering the familial relationship as a factor that could undermine the objectivity of the witness's statements Bias 
in the Affidavit for Arrest: Basic Element: Integrity and truthfulness of statements leading to an arrest Legal Concern: Whether the bias compromised the affidavit's reliability, potentially violating constitutional rights Procedural Analysis: A challenge to the 
affidavit based on alleged bias could lead to a suppression hearing (Franks v. Delaware), where the defense can argue that the initial bias taints the arrest and any evidence derived from it, thus inadmissible Relevant Cases Brady v. Maryland | 373 U.S. 83 (1963) 
 Obligation to disclose exculpatory evidence Highlights the importance of fairness in proceedings relevant to disclosing biases Franks v. Delaware | 438 U.S. 154 (1978) Right to challenge false statements in warrant affidavits | Provides a procedural avenue 
for addressing bias in the affidavit for arrest Conclusion The scenario's analysis, grounded in First Principles Thinking, underscores the critical importance of ensuring procedural integrity and the accused's constitutional rights The detective's familial relationship 
with a witness and the bias in the affidavit for arrest introduce significant legal challenges that must be navigated with a deep understanding of constitutional protections, evidence standards, and the overarching principle of fairness in the criminal justice system 
 Violations and Illegal Acts: a) Brady Violations: The prosecution appears to have committed several Brady violations by failing to disclose exculpatory evidence In Brady v. Maryland, 373 U.S. 83 (1963), the Supreme Court held that the suppression of evidence favorable to 
an accused violates due process where the evidence is material either to guilt or to punishment, irrespective of the good faith or bad faith of the prosecution b) Perjury and Subornation of Perjury: There are strong indications of perjury by multiple witnesses and potential 
subornation of perjury by the prosecution 14-209, perjury is a serious offense In North Carolina, malicious prosecution requires proof of: (1) the defendant initiated the earlier proceeding; (2) malice on the part of the defendant in doing so; (3) lack of 
probable cause for the initiation of the earlier proceeding; and (4) termination of the earlier proceeding in favor of the plaintiff d) Civil Rights Violations: The actions of law enforcement and the prosecution may constitute violations of Mr. Allen's civil rights under 42 
U.S.C This statute allows individuals to sue state government employees and others acting "under color of state law " for civil rights violations Potential violations include: Unlawful arrest without probable cause Malicious prosecution Fabrication of 
evidence e) Prosecutorial Misconduct: The ADA's actions, including offering a plea deal based on false information and potentially presenting false evidence to the grand jury, may constitute prosecutorial misconduct In Berger v. United States, 295 U.S. 78 (1935), the Supreme 
Court held that a prosecutor's interest in a criminal case is not that they shall win, but that justice shall be done As we delve deeper into these violations and illegal acts, it's important to consider the systemic implications How might these actions reflect broader 
issues within the local justice system? , As we delve into these aspects, it's crucial to consider how seemingly minor missteps or oversights can compound, leading to significant miscarriages of justice We must also consider the potential motivations behind these actions - 
whether they stem from incompetence, bias, or more deliberate attempts to manipulate the justice system Relevant case law: In Miller v. Pate, 386 U.S. 1 (1967), the Supreme Court held that the Fourteenth Amendment cannot tolerate a state criminal conviction obtained by the 
knowing use of false evidence This is a severe violation of ethical standards and potentially constitutes fraud upon the court Relevant case law: In Napue v. Illinois, 360 U.S. 264 (1959), the Court held that the Fourteenth Amendment is violated when the State, although 
not soliciting false evidence, allows it to go uncorrected when it appears This failure to secure and analyze physical evidence is a significant deviation from standard investigative procedures f) Inadequate Documentation: The lack of proper documentation, including 
incomplete or inaccurate reports, and the absence of a clear chain of custody for evidence, indicates substandard investigative practices These investigative failures raise serious questions about the training and oversight of law enforcement in this jurisdiction How 
might systemic issues contribute to such widespread neglect of proper procedures? , As we examine these aspects, it's important to consider how adherence to proper investigative procedures serves not only to protect the rights of the accused but also to ensure the integrity of 
the justice system as a whole We must also consider how the failure to follow these procedures can undermine public trust in law enforcement and the courts This includes: Failure to secure and document the scene properly Lack of systematic evidence 
collection Failure to take comprehensive photographs of the alleged crime scene Neglect in collecting potential DNA evidence from bedding and other relevant surfaces Relevant standard: The National Institute of Justice's "Crime Scene Investigation: A Guide for Law 
Enforcement " provides detailed procedures that should have been followed Potential Violations of State and Federal Laws: a) Fourth Amendment Violations: The arrest of Mr. Allen without proper probable cause may constitute a violation of his Fourth Amendment rights 
against unreasonable seizures Case law: In Henry v. United States, 361 U.S. 98 (1959), the Supreme Court held that probable cause exists if the facts and circumstances known to the officer warrant a prudent man in believing that an offense has been committed b) 
Fourteenth Amendment Due Process Violations: The numerous issues with the investigation and prosecution, including Brady violations and the use of false evidence, likely constitute violations of Mr. Allen's Fourteenth Amendment right to due process Case law: In United States 
v. Agurs, 427 U.S. 97 (1976), the Court held that the prosecution's duty to disclose evidence encompasses impeachment evidence as well as exculpatory evidence d) North Carolina General Statute 15A-903 - Disclosure of Evidence by the State: The prosecution's failure 
to disclose exculpatory evidence and potential manipulation of evidence likely violates this statute, which requires the state to provide the defendant with all materials and information in its possession that are favorable to the defendant e) North Carolina Rules of

prosecutors $antthinking$ The extent and nature of these overlooked procedures and potential legal violations suggest a systemic failure in the local justice system It raises questions about the adequacy of training, the effectiveness of oversight mechanisms, and the

the balance between public safety concerns and individual rights We must scrutinize each element that contributed to the decision to arrest Mr. Allen, considering both the letter of the law and the spirit of justice it aims to uphold Probable Cause Analysis: Legal 
Standard for Probable Cause: Probable cause is defined as a reasonable belief that a person has committed a crime The U.S. Supreme Court in Illinois v. Gates, 462 U.S. 213 (1983), established that probable cause is determined based on the "totality of the 
circumstances , This requires a practical, common-sense decision whether, given all the circumstances, there is a fair probability that a crime has been committed and that the suspect committed it As stated in State v. Biber, 365 N.C. 162, 712 S.E.2d 874 (2011), 
 "Probable cause requires only a probability or substantial chance of criminal activity, not an actual showing of such activity , Examination of Factors Considered for Probable Cause: a) Accuser's Statement: Lauren Deal's statement was the primary basis for the 
arrest However, several issues undermine its reliability: Inconsistencies in her account, particularly regarding the timeline of events Her admitted consumption of alcohol, which could affect her recollection The lack of immediate reporting, which allowed time for 
potential fabrication or influence from others Case law: In State v. Knoll, 322 N.C. 535, 369 S.E.2d 558 (1988), the North Carolina Supreme Court held that an officer must consider the totality of the circumstances, including the credibility of the complainant, when determining 
probable cause 770, 803 S.E.2d 419 (2017), the court emphasized the importance of considering all available evidence, including exculpatory evidence, in determining probable cause Case law: In Franks v. Delaware, 438 U.S. 154 (1978), the Supreme Court held that a 
defendant is entitled to a hearing if they can make a substantial preliminary showing that a false statement knowingly and intentionally, or with reckless disregard for the truth, was included in a warrant affidavit This conflict of interest taints the entire process In 
State v. Campbell, 282 N.C. 125, 191 S.E.2d 752 (1972), the North Carolina Supreme Court emphasized the importance of corroborating evidence in establishing probable cause 1999), the court held that an officer has a duty to conduct a reasonably thorough investigation prior to 
arresting a suspect, including consideration of exculpatory evidence $antthinking$ The numerous flaws in the probable cause determination in this case point to a systemic issue in how law enforcement and prosecutors approach sexual assault allegations While it's crucial 
to take such allegations seriously, this case illustrates the dangers of abandoning due diligence and the presumption of innocence in the pursuit of a quick arrest How might we balance the need to support victims with the constitutional rights of the accused? , This failure 
to establish proper probable cause has significant implications for the case: The arrest itself may be deemed unlawful, potentially leading to the suppression of any evidence obtained as a result of the arrest under the "fruit of the poisonous tree " doctrine It calls 
into question the integrity of the entire investigation and subsequent prosecution, potentially leading to dismissal of all charges It highlights the need for better training and oversight in law enforcement agencies to prevent similar miscarriages of justice in the future 
 As we consider these remedies and strategies, it's crucial to balance aggressive defense tactics with an awareness of the broader implications for the justice system We must consider not only what will best serve Mr. Allen's interests, but also how these actions might 
contribute to systemic improvements in law enforcement and prosecutorial practices Potential Remedies and Defense Strategies: Motion to Suppress Evidence: Given the lack of probable cause for Mr. Allen's arrest, a motion to suppress all evidence obtained as a result of the 
arrest should be filed under the "fruit of the poisonous tree " doctrine established in Wong Sun v. United States, 371 U.S. 471 (1963) Strategy: Argue that the arrest was unlawful due to lack of probable cause, and therefore any evidence obtained as a result, including 
statements made by Mr. Allen and physical evidence collected, should be excluded from trial Case law: In State v. Gant, 361 N.C. 389, 646 S.E.2d 727 (2007), the North Carolina Supreme Court held that evidence obtained as a result of an unlawful arrest must be suppressed 
 Motion to Dismiss Charges: File a motion to dismiss all charges based on the violation of Mr. Allen's constitutional rights and the lack of credible evidence supporting the charges Strategy: Argue that the combination of Fourth Amendment violations (unlawful arrest), Brady 
violations, and prosecutorial misconduct has irreparably prejudiced Mr. Allen's right to a fair trial Case law: In State v. Lamb, 321 N.C. 633, 365 S.E.2d 600 (1988), the North Carolina Supreme Court held that dismissal is appropriate when there has been irreparable prejudice 
to the defendant's preparation of his case Legal Analysis: Probable cause requires facts and circumstances that would lead a reasonable person to believe a crime has been committed The standard is lower than beyond reasonable doubt but higher than mere suspicion 
 Case Law: Illinois v. Gates, 462 U.S. 213 (1983) established the "totality of the circumstances " test for probable cause Beck v. Ohio, 379 U.S. 89 (1964) held that probable cause exists when "the facts and circumstances within the officers' knowledge and of which 
they had reasonably trustworthy information are sufficient in themselves to warrant a man of reasonable caution in the belief that an offense has been or is being committed , This is an excellent question that requires careful analysis of Miranda doctrine and its application 
to custodial interrogations To properly assess when Miranda warnings should have been given to Ben Allen, we need to consider several key Supreme Court cases and their implications First, let's review the foundational case: Miranda v. Arizona, 384 U.S. 436 
(1966) Established that police must inform suspects of their rights before a custodial interrogation Rights include: right to remain silent, right to an attorney, warning that statements can be used against them The key question here is whether Ben Allen was in "custody " 
for Miranda purposes during this interview The test for custody is not simply whether the person is at a police station, but whether a reasonable person would feel free to leave Relevant cases on custody determination: Oregon v. Mathiason, 429 U.S. 492 
(1977) Voluntary arrival at police station and brief questioning did not constitute custody Court emphasized importance of coercive environment, not just location California v. Beheler, 463 U.S. 1121 (1983) Reinforced that Miranda only applies to custodial 
interrogation Voluntary interview at police station without arrest was not custodial Thompson v. Keohane, 516 U.S. 99 (1995) Outlined two-part inquiry for custody: a) Circumstances surrounding interrogation b) Given those circumstances, would a reasonable person have felt free 
to terminate the interrogation and leave Yarborough v. Alvarado, 541 U.S. 652 (2004) Custody determination should be based on objective circumstances, not subjective views of the interrogating officers or person being questioned Applying these principles to Ben Allen's 
situation: He came to the station voluntarily The conversation seems relatively casual at the start He expresses concerns about his job, suggesting he's thinking about consequences There's no indication he was told he couldn't leave However, the turning point may be when 
Detective Freeman starts asking specific questions about the night's events At this point, the interaction becomes more of an interrogation about potential criminal activity Berkemer v. McCarty, 468 U.S. 420 (1984) held that Miranda applies when a suspect is subjected to 
restraints comparable to those associated with formal arrest The key is whether there's a "formal arrest or restraint on freedom of movement of the degree associated with formal arrest , It's worth noting that failure to give Miranda warnings doesn't necessarily 
make the arrest itself unlawful, but it could result in suppression of any incriminating statements made during the interrogation United States v. Patane, 542 U.S. 630 (2004) held that physical evidence obtained as a result of unwarned statements is still admissible, absent 
actual coercion The exact point is somewhat subjective, but err on the side of caution to protect the admissibility of any statements made This is an important question that requires careful consideration of legal ethics, professional standards for law enforcement, and 
the broader implications for justice and due process Let's analyze the detective's behavior through multiple lenses: Legal and Procedural Ethics: Miranda Warnings: As discussed previously, the detective should have given Miranda warnings once the conversation turned 
into a focused interrogation about potential criminal activity Failure to do so could be seen as an attempt to circumvent constitutional protections This lack of transparency could be seen as manipulative and potentially violating the spirit, if not the letter, of laws 
designed to protect suspects' rights This ambiguity could be seen as a form of subtle coercion Professional Ethics: Duty of Candor: Law enforcement officers have an ethical duty to be truthful and transparent in their dealings with the public The detective's 
approach here seems to skirt the edges of this duty Abuse of Power: By leveraging the casual nature of the conversation to elicit information without proper warnings, the detective could be seen as abusing his position of authority Investigative Integrity: While the 
detective is gathering information, his methods could potentially compromise the integrity of the investigation if key statements are later deemed inadmissible Ethical Frameworks: Utilitarian Perspective: One could argue that the detective's actions serve the greater good 
by potentially solving a crime However, this view ignores the long-term societal costs of eroding trust in law enforcement and potentially convicting someone without due process Deontological Ethics: From a duty-based ethical standpoint, the detective's actions are 
problematic Virtue Ethics: The detective's behavior doesn't align well with virtues we'd hope to see in law enforcement, such as honesty, transparency, and respect for individual rights Legal Precedents: In Spano v. New York, 360 U.S. 315 (1959), the Supreme Court 
emphasized the importance of fair interrogation practices, stating: "The abhorrence of society to the use of involuntary confessions does not turn alone on their inherent untrustworthiness It also turns on the deep-rooted feeling that the police must obey the law while 
enforcing the law , This sentiment underscores the ethical imperative for law enforcement to adhere to high standards of conduct, even when investigating serious crimes Conclusion: While the detective's actions may not rise to the level of overt misconduct, they do 
raise significant ethical concerns The approach taken appears to prioritize information gathering over respect for the suspect's rights and the integrity of the legal process This type of behavior, if widespread, could undermine public trust in law enforcement and the 
justice system as a whole A more ethical approach would involve: Clearly informing Allen of his status as a suspect Providing Miranda warnings at the appropriate time Ensuring Allen understands he's free to leave Being more transparent about the nature and purpose 
of the questioning Ultimately, while effective law enforcement is crucial for public safety, it must be balanced with respect for individual rights and the ethical principles that underpin our justice system The detective's behavior in this case falls short of the high 
ethical standards we should expect from law enforcement professionals This analysis provides a comprehensive breakdown of the appellate court's decision, examining the factual background, procedural history, legal issues, court's holdings, and the reasoning behind the

Reference This report on the Benjamin Allen case file uncovers a disturbing panorama of failures within the criminal justice system, demanding an urgent and thorough investigation The extensive misconduct and procedural violations detailed in this report are profoundly 
concerning, illustrating both isolated errors and a systemic failure of justice This is not a mere oversight; it strikes at the heart of impartiality, a cornerstone of any legitimate investigation As Justice Louis Brandeis articulated in Olmstead v. United States, 
a landmark case on the Fourth Amendment and the right to privacy, "Decency, security, and liberty alike demand that government officials shall be subjected to the same rules of conduct that are commands to the citizen , This directly violates the principle of due process, 
as the very foundation of the investigation becomes suspect This selective investigation echoes the concerns raised by Justice Black , consistently warning against shortcuts and biases in law enforcement that undermine individual liberties Crime Scene Neglect: The 
report meticulously documents the egregious failures in crime scene processing This is not a technicality but a fundamental failure to adhere to established investigative standards, as meticulously outlined in crime scene investigation protocols As Justice Holmes 
famously stated, "The life of the law has not been logic; it has been experience , Lack of Authentication: The documented breaches in the chain of custody, coupled with the failure to authenticate crucial pieces of evidence like the pants allegedly belonging to the 
accuser, raise serious questions about the integrity and admissibility of evidence presented at trial Without a proper chain of custody and authentication, the evidentiary value of these items is severely compromised, undermining the foundation of the prosecution's 
narrative This is not merely a procedural oversight; it directly affects the reliability and trustworthiness of the evidence upon which a trial can be held This directly implicates Brady v. Maryland The cumulative effect of these false statements and 
omissions undermines the probable cause upon which the arrest and subsequent prosecution were predicated Brady Violations Probable Cause Defects Procedural Omissions False Timeline Reporting Legal Requirement for Reporting: In North Carolina, hospitals are legally 
obligated to report cases of suspected assault, abuse, or other injuries that may involve criminal activity Failure to report such cases to law enforcement can result in felony charges against the hospital staff or administration This mandatory reporting requirement 
ensures that law enforcement is promptly notified in cases involving potential crimes, thereby enabling immediate investigation and evidence preservation If CCH failed to report the incident to law enforcement, or if they did report but records are now being withheld, this 
could represent a serious breach of legal obligations and raise questions about collusion or mishandling Legal Consequences for Non-Compliance: If it is found that CCH did not fulfill its reporting obligation, it could be subject to legal consequences, including potential 
felony charges Furthermore, failing to comply with reporting laws raises suspicion that hospital staff, law enforcement, or both may have had a reason to avoid documenting this case correctly An arrangement between hospital staff and law enforcement to circumvent 
standard reporting protocols would be highly irregular and potentially unlawful, implying a deliberate effort to delay or obscure initial investigatory actions Withholding these records is an attempt to obstruct justice as law enforcement and hospital staff sought to avoid 
initiating a formal investigation to protect individuals involved Possibility of Collusion: A deliberate attempt to avoid creating a formal record of the incident indicates collusion between hospital staff and law enforcement to keep the case off the books until a more 
convenient time This collusion is a serious breach of ethical and legal standards, indicating that personal interests and relationships influenced official duties The arrangement was made to postpone investigatory action, which constitutes obstruction of justice and has 
serious legal ramifications for all parties involved The failure to release 911 records and other relevant documentation is grounds for questioning the validity of the initial investigation and the defendant's arrest If these records continue to be withheld, the defense 
should seek a subpoena to compel their release Prosecutorial Misconduct The conduct of the Assistant District Attorney (ADA) detailed in this report is equally, if not more, alarming It reveals a pattern of behavior that suggests a conscious disregard for ethical 
obligations and due process, actively compounding the investigative failures This is not aggressive advocacy, but outright deception intended to coerce a guilty plea under false pretenses In Miranda v. Arizona, a case that established the principle of the right to 
remain silent, Justice Warren emphasized the importance of protecting individuals against coercion within the legal system The ADA's actions violate this principle, exploit the defendant's vulnerability, and undermine the integrity of the plea-bargaining process, potentially 
leading to a wrongful conviction Withholding exculpatory evidence and failing to disclose critical material directly violate Brady obligations The cumulative effect of these actions systematically disadvantages the defense, undermining their ability to 
prepare a strong case and effectively challenge the prosecution's narrative This procedural manipulation directly infringes upon the defendant's Sixth Amendment right to effective counsel and a fair trial, highlighting the systemic issues in the case, where both the 
investigative and prosecutorial sides appear to be working against the defendant Discovery Violations: Procedural Abuse: The egregious delay of thirty-seven continuances over four years is not merely inefficiency; it constitutes a deliberate abuse of the legal

Carolina Law Mandating Unanimous Jury Verdicts The legal framework of North Carolina robustly protects the right to a unanimous jury verdict in criminal proceedings Section 24 of Article I of the Constitution of North Carolina underpins this right, ensuring that no 
individual can be convicted of a crime without the unanimous consent of a jury of their peers General Statute 15A-1201(a) explicitly mandates that, absent a waiver by the defendant pursuant to 15A-1201(b), a defendant in a criminal trial possesses the right to 
be tried by a jury of twelve, and that the verdict rendered by this jury must be unanimous Further procedural safeguards are detailed in General Statute 15A-1237, which prescribes the essential elements of a valid criminal verdict This statute dictates that all 
criminal verdicts must adhere to the following criteria: Written Form: The verdict must be documented in writing Foreperson Signature: The written verdict must be signed by the jury foreperson Unanimity: The verdict must reflect the unanimous 
agreement of all jurors Open Court Return: The verdict must be formally returned by the jury in open court Record Inclusion: The verdict must be officially incorporated into the court record The legislative intent behind the written verdict requirement, 
as elucidated in the Official Commentary to G.S 15A-1237 and State v. Goodman, 298 N.C. 1 (1979), is to mitigate potential inaccuracies and omissions that could occur during the oral pronouncement of a verdict by the foreperson However, North Carolina jurisprudence has 
also recognized that strict adherence to procedural formalities should not supersede the precise substance of a jury's decision 685 (2004), verdicts have been upheld despite minor procedural oversights, indicating a pragmatic approach where the core elements of a valid verdict 
are demonstrably present De Facto Acquittal Established by Jury Communication in the Allen Case The official court transcript of the Allen case proceedings contains a critical exchange that firmly supports the argument for a de facto acquittal on the first-degree 
forcible rape charge The relevant excerpt is as follows: [The jury entered the courtroom and were seated in jury box] THE COURT: All right However, we're split nine to three on the lesser charge of second-degree forcible rape Read in open court FOR THE 
RECORD This exchange reveals that the jury foreperson submitted a written communication, bearing his signature, to the presiding judge Upon receiving this written communication, the judge acknowledged its written and signed nature in open court and, significantly, read 
it aloud "FOR THE RECORD , This judicial action of reading the jury's note into the official record is paramount While the jury did not utilize a pre-printed verdict form designated explicitly for a "not guilty " verdict on first-degree rape, their written, 
signed communication, delivered through the foreperson and officially recorded by the court, substantively fulfills the core requirements of G.S The written note, signed by the foreperson, unequivocally declares the unanimous decision of the jury regarding the first-degree 
rape charge The explicit statement by the judge to record this communication further solidifies its official status within the trial proceedings Drawing upon the pragmatic approach adopted by North Carolina courts, as exemplified in State v. 
Collins and State v. Simmons, a rigid adherence to a specific verdict form should not eclipse the clear and officially documented unanimous decision of the jury 15A-1237, to ensure accuracy and prevent errors, is demonstrably met by the written jury note that was 
formally integrated into the court record Fundamental Principles of Double Jeopardy The principle of double jeopardy, deeply rooted in Anglo-American jurisprudence, dictates that an individual shall not be twice placed in jeopardy for the same offense This protection 
is designed to prevent the state, with its vast resources, from repeatedly attempting to convict an individual for an alleged crime The fundamental tenet of double jeopardy is that a verdict of acquittal, once rendered, is final and cannot be overturned or ignored To 
subject Mr. Allen to a retrial on the charge of first-degree forcible rape, after the jury in his initial trial unequivocally communicated their unanimous "not guilty " decision to the court and had it officially recorded, would directly contravene these foundational 
principles Such an action would undermine the finality of jury decisions and erode the protections afforded by the double jeopardy clause The defendant possesses a valued right to have their trial concluded by a particular tribunal For the foregoing reasons, we 
respectfully submit that the proceedings in the initial trial of Benjamin Allen established a de facto acquittal on the charge of first-degree forcible rape While emphasizing procedural safeguards for jury verdicts, North Carolina law also recognizes the primacy of 
the jury's substantive decision The jury's written, signed note, read into the record in open court, clearly and unequivocally expresses their unanimous determination of not guilty on the first-degree rape charge Retrying Mr. Allen on this charge would violate the 
fundamental protections against double jeopardy under North Carolina law and broader principles of justice We therefore urge the Court to recognize the de facto acquittal and dismiss the charge of first-degree forcible rape with prejudice, ensuring that Mr. Allen 
is not subjected to the injustice of being placed twice in jeopardy for an offense for which he has already been acquitted Conclusion: A Clarion Call for Justice and Systemic Reform The Benjamin Allen case is not merely a chronicle of errors and missteps; it is a damning

arrests, which include arrests made without a reasonable basis Probable cause forms the threshold that must be met before depriving an individual of liberty, serving as a check on law enforcement power to ensure that any interference with personal freedom is legally 
justified Probable Cause Documentation as a Procedural Safeguard Documentation as Evidence of Legitimacy: Documenting probable cause is crucial in establishing the legitimacy of an arrest It provides a written record for the court, the suspect, and the public to 
understand the factual basis of the detention This documentation is essential because it allows for judicial review, where a judge or magistrate can evaluate the lawfulness of the arrest Written Record and Chain of Justification: Probable cause documentation is typically 
written in an affidavit or report form and is presented to a magistrate or judge when requesting an arrest warrant This ensures a chain of justification, showing a clear, recorded rationale behind the arrest that can be reviewed, scrutinized, or contested in 
court if necessary Implications of Not Documenting Probable Cause Impact on Judicial Review: In the absence of probable cause documentation, there is no record for a judge or magistrate to assess the validity of the detention Judicial review is a cornerstone of the

legal grounds of his detention Potential Constitutional Concerns: A failure to document probable cause can lead to constitutional concerns, as it challenges the transparency and legitimacy of an arrest Such a challenge could result in a motion to suppress evidence 
gathered following the arrest, potentially weakening the prosecution case Consequences in Criminal Proceedings Evidentiary Issues: Lack of probable cause documentation can lead to evidentiary challenges For instance, evidence obtained following an arrest

a reasonable basis for believing that a person has committed a crime, established through facts and evidence rather than speculation or personal judgment Probable cause acts as a safeguard against arbitrary detention, ensuring that arrests are based on verifiable information 
and protecting citizens Fourth Amendment rights Legal Importance of Probable Cause: The U.S. Constitution Fourth Amendment establishes that arrests should only be conducted when probable cause exists, either through an officer direct observations, 
reliable witness accounts, or other corroborative evidence For an arrest to be lawful, the evidence supporting probable cause must be substantive enough to withstand judicial review, typically documented in detail within an affidavit Typically, officers create a written 
record or affidavit outlining the basis for probable cause, especially in cases where the arrest is made without a warrant This document serves as a transparent and verifiable record for judicial review Legal Standards and Precedents on Probable Cause 
Documentation Case Law Supporting Written Documentation: Gerstein v. Pugh (1975): This case emphasized that the Constitution requires a prompt judicial determination of probable cause for any significant detention Without written documentation, the court lacks a reliable 
basis for validating the lawfulness of detention Illinois v. Gates (1983): This case addressed the totality of the circumstances in establishing probable cause, but it also emphasized the importance of having a documented record that a judge can review, ensuring 
that the arrest was based on objective facts rather than subjective judgment If there is no verifiable record of probable cause, the arrest may lack the constitutionally required basis, which could lead to significant procedural challenges Officers in his position are 
not only expected to know these requirements but are often responsible for teaching them, making his omission more notable Adherence to Probable Cause Standards: Law enforcement officers are trained to document probable cause, especially in cases where the arrest occurs 
without a warrant Implications of Probable Cause Issues on Allen Arrest Risk of Unlawful Detention: Without documented probable cause, Allen detention could be seen as lacking a legal foundation If the arrest cannot be shown to have met the probable 
cause threshold, the detention could be deemed unlawful, infringing upon Allen Fourth Amendment rights This would make all subsequent evidence potentially vulnerable to suppression Fruit of the Poisonous Tree Doctrine: If Allen detention is found unlawful 
due to insufficient probable cause documentation, the defense may invoke the fruit of the poisonous tree doctrine This doctrine holds that any evidence obtained from an illegal detention is tainted and should be excluded from the trial In this case, the 
absence of documented probable cause weakens the arrest and could endanger evidence collected thereafter Defense Challenge Based on Constitutional Grounds: Allen defense could argue that his detention violated constitutional protections due to the lack of written 
probable cause How the Retrospective Order Affects Probable Cause The Magistrate Retrospective Justification: The magistrate order indicated that Allen was arrested without a warrant and that the detention was justified by probable cause 
 This order retrospectively legitimizes the detention but does not provide the same legal foundation as a pre-detention warrant based on documented probable cause Procedural and Legal Limitations of Retrospective Orders: Retrospective approval can only serve as a stopgap and 
does not replace the necessity of documented probable cause By approving the arrest after it had occurred without a formal written record, the magistrate order could still be subject to challenge, especially if the court finds that it circumvented standard judicial 
review protocols An officer credibility is central to the prosecution case, and any appearance of procedural shortcuts or omissions can erode confidence in the investigation integrity This document would undergo judicial review before or 
immediately after custody, ensuring transparency and legal compliance For an experienced officer, this is an unusual and legally vulnerable approach, suggesting either an intentional bypass of protocol or an assumption that his judgment would suffice Defense Strategy and 
Possible Legal Challenges Motion to Suppress Evidence: The defense could file a motion to suppress evidence obtained post-detention, arguing that the lack of documented probable cause invalidates the arrest If the court agrees, key evidence collected following Allen

constitutional standards, citing cases like Gerstein and McLaughlin that require prompt judicial review based on documented evidence The magistrate retrospective approval does not fully replace the need for pre-detention documentation, as it bypasses the usual review 
processes and lacks the rigor of a documented affidavit If you have further questions or would like to explore additional aspects of this case, feel free to let me know This could undermine the prosecution argument that the physical evidence conclusively supports

factual evidence This document is presented to a judge or magistrate to demonstrate the need to search a particular location based on evidence directly connected to the case Probable cause affidavits are foundational to the integrity of the judicial review process, as 
they allow judges to make impartial decisions based on verified information Probable cause affidavits are held to high standards of truthfulness and accuracy Fabrications or exaggerations in affidavits not only jeopardize the specific search warrant but can also reflect 
poorly on the integrity of the entire investigation, potentially leading to claims of investigational bias or misconduct Adding unverified narratives to a probable cause affidavit can also open the door to suppression motions If the defense can demonstrate that the 
affidavit contained embellishments or falsehoods, they could argue that the search warrant was issued on improper grounds Potential Legal Consequences: Suppression of Evidence: If the court finds that the search warrant was based on an inaccurate affidavit, any evidence 
obtained from the search could be suppressed This doctrine, known as the fruit of the poisonous tree, applies when initial misconduct (e.g., false information in an affidavit) taints subsequent evidence collection Absence of a Probable Cause Affidavit for

ensuring that the detention is lawful and based on verified evidence Probable cause affidavits for arrest are essential for accountability They offer a clear, documented rationale for the arrest, which allows for judicial review, protects the detainee rights, and 
establishes transparency in the arrest process This absence suggests a potential violation of Allen Fourth Amendment rights, as the arrest lacks the formal documentation needed to justify the detention In court, this omission could be interpreted as a procedural

Analysis.docx Report on Critical Issues within the Criminal Justice System: Analysis of Benjamin Allen Case File Executive Summary This report presents a comprehensive analysis of the Allen case file, revealing numerous critical failures and misconduct throughout 
various stages of the criminal justice process The identified issues encompass judicial oversight, investigative procedures, prosecutorial conduct, evidence handling, defense representation, and systemic vulnerabilities These failures raise significant concerns about due 
process, fair trial rights, and the potential for wrongful convictions This document underscores specific procedural lapses, ethical violations, and intentional misconduct, highlighting the urgent need for systemic reforms and a thorough case review Investigative 
Failures and Detective Misconduct Benjamin S. Allen Case: Systemic Breakdown This case is emblematic of a profound systemic failure characterized by alleged misconduct, significant investigative lapses, and ineffective legal counsel Detective Bias: The investigation 
was demonstrably biased, compromising the impartiality and thoroughness expected in criminal proceedings Evidence Mishandling: Evidence mishandling raises questions about the integrity and reliability of the evidence presented Formal Misconduct Complaints: To 
underscore the severity of the ethical and procedural concerns, formal complaints must be filed against the investigating detective and Assistant District Attorney for professional misconduct Systemic Reform Imperative: These failures are not isolated incidents but point 
to systemic issues within the Lee County Sherrif Department and Lee County judicial frameworks, necessitating broader reforms to prevent future miscarriages of justice This situation presents serious constitutional and legal challenges Prosecutorial 
Misconduct ADA (Assistant District Attorney) Issues: Ethical Lapses The Assistant District Attorney's (ADA) conduct reveals multiple instances of prosecutorial misconduct, undermining the fairness and impartiality of the legal process Procedural Abuse: Excessive 
Continuances: The case was subjected to excessive continuances 37 times over four years constituting procedural abuse and causing undue hardship to the defendant, including the loss of evidence critical to the case Discovery Violations: Discovery 
violations further prejudiced against the defendant's defense ability Due Process Violations: Fair Trial Infringement Cumulative Violations: The combination of discovery violations, lost evidence, and excessive delays collectively infringed upon the defendant's 
fundamental right to a fair trial and due process as guaranteed by the Constitution Evidence Collection and Handling Issues Serious Evidence Collection Failures: Procedural Negligence Significant failures in evidence collection and handling highlight systemic weaknesses in 
investigative procedures Crime Scene Neglect: Failure to collect evidence from crime scenes and inadequate crime-scene investigation procedures demonstrate a lack of thoroughness Chain of Custody Problems: Breakdowns in the chain of custody for evidence 
compromise the integrity and admissibility of collected materials Defense Representation Issues Ineffective Assistance of Counsel: Attorney Deficiencies The defendant suffered from ineffective assistance of counsel, significantly undermining their ability to receive a fair 
defense Jury Impact: These failures negatively impacted the jury's verdict, prejudicing the trial's outcome Attorney Conflict of Interest: A critical conflict of interest existed because the attorney had a previous relationship with the detective, which 
compromised the attorney's impartiality and effectiveness Defense Representation Issues Ineffective Assistance of Counsel In this case, erroneous convictions were not due to single errors but resulted from a compounding effect of initial investigative mistakes exacerbated 
by subsequent failures throughout the legal process Inadequate Defense Preparation: Defense attorneys failed to prepare adequate defenses, weakening the defendant's position Prosecutorial Misconduct Following Initial Errors: Prosecutorial misconduct 
compounded the initial investigative errors, creating a cascade of injustice Brady Violations: Exculpatory Evidence Suppression Brady violations refer to instances where prosecutors fail to disclose material evidence favorable to the defendant, as established by the landmark 
Supreme Court case Brady v. Maryland (373 U.S. 83, 1963) This ruling established that suppression of evidence favorable to an accused violates due process when the evidence is material either to guilt or punishment, regardless of the prosecution's good faith or bad faith 
 Key Legal Principles Related to Brady Violations Basic Obligation: The prosecution must disclose all favorable, material evidence to the defense, even without a specific request from the defendant Types of Evidence That Must Be Disclosed: Evidence that tends to negate 
guilt Evidence that mitigates an offense or potential sentence Impeachment evidence that affects witness credibility Evidence discrediting police investigation or officers' credibility Materiality Standard: Evidence is considered material "if there is a reasonable 
probability that, had the evidence been disclosed to the defense, the result of the proceeding would have been different " (United States v. Bagley, 473 U.S. 667, 682, 1985) Timing of Disclosure: The prosecution must provide Brady material in time for the defendant to 
effectively use it at trial Evidence Need Not Be Admissible: The prosecution must disclose favorable, material evidence, even if it would be inadmissible at trial, as long as it could lead to admissible evidence Legal Basis: Napue v. Illinois established that prosecutors 
violate defendants' due process rights under the Fourteenth Amendment when allowing false testimony to go uncorrected Misuse of Grand Jury Processes The ADA persists in employing grand jury practices explicitly declared unconstitutional, such as introducing misleading 
evidence and false testimony Legal Basis: Misleading a grand jury or knowingly introducing false evidence violates a defendant's Fifth Amendment right to due process and fair proceedings Such misuse directly undermines the integrity of the judicial process, constituting 
actionable prosecutorial misconduct under 1983 Violation of Established Brady Obligations The prosecutors' obligations to disclose exculpatory evidence (Brady v. Maryland), the ADA deliberately withholds such evidence, depriving defendants of their right to a fair 
trial Legal Basis: Withholding exculpatory evidence violates Brady v. Maryland and subsequent decisions (e.g., Giglio v. United States), infringing on defendants Fourteenth Amendment rights to due process and fair trial Prosecutorial misconduct of this type is 
explicitly actionable Failure to Comply with Declared Right to Speedy Trial The ADA denied the defendant right to a speedy trial by unnecessarily delaying, depriving the defendant of timely adjudication and fundamental procedural fairness Legal Basis: The Sixth

seizures, shall not be violated, and no Warrants shall issue but upon probable cause, supported by Oath or affirmation, and particularly describing the place to be searched, and the persons or things to be seized Annotations First Amendment Bearing on Probable Cause and 
Particularity u2014Where the warrant process is used to authorize seizure of books and other items that may be protected by the First Amendment, the Court has required government to observe more exacting standards than in other cases.135 Seizure of materials arguably protected by 
the First Amendment is a form of prior restraint that requires strict observance of the Fourth Amendment At a minimum, a warrant is required, and additional safeguards may be required for large-scale seizures Thus, in Marcus v. Search Warrant,136 the seizure of 11,000 
copies of 280 publications pursuant to warrant issued ex parte by a magistrate who had not examined any of the publications but who had relied on the conclusory affidavit of a policeman was voided Failure to scrutinize the materials and to particularize the items to be seized 
was deemed inadequate, and it was further noted that police were provided with no guide to the exercise of informed discretion, because there was no step in the procedure before seizure designed to focus searchingly on the question of obscenity 137 A state procedure 
that was designed to comply with Marcus by the presentation of copies of books to be seized to the magistrate for his scrutiny prior to issuance of a warrant was nonetheless found inadequate by a plurality of the Court, which concluded that since the warrant here authorized 
the sheriff to seize all copies of the specified titles, and since appellant was not afforded a hearing on the question of the obscenity even of the seven novels seven of 59 listed titles were reviewed by the magistrate before the warrant issued, the procedure was 
 constitutionally deficient 138 Confusion remains, however, about the necessity for and the character of prior adversary hearings on the issue of obscenity In a later decision the Court held that, with adequate safeguards, no preseizure adversary hearing on the issue 
of obscenity is required if the film is seized not for the purpose of destruction as contraband (the purpose in Marcus and A Quantity of Books), but instead to preserve a copy for evidence.139 It is constitutionally permissible to seize a copy of a film pursuant to a warrant as long 
as there is a prompt post-seizure adversary hearing on the obscenity issue Until there is a judicial determination of obscenity, the Court advised, the film may continue to be exhibited; if no other copy is available either a copy of it must be made from the seized film or the 
film itself must be returned.140 The seizure of a film without the authority of a constitutionally sufficient warrant is invalid; seizure cannot be justified as incidental to arrest, as the determination of obscenity may not be made by the officer himself.141 Nor may a warrant 
issue based solely on the conclusory assertions of the police officer without any inquiry by the magistrate into the factual basis for the officer conclusions 142 Instead, a warrant must be supported by affidavits setting forth specific facts in order 
that the issuing magistrate may focus searchingly on the question of obscenity u2019 143 This does not mean, however, that a higher standard of probable cause is required in order to obtain a warrant to seize materials protected by the First Amendment of 
reasonableness was not intended to establish a higher standard of probable cause for the issuance of a warrant to seize books or films, but instead related to the more basic requirement, imposed by that decision, that the police not rely on the 
 exigency exception to the Fourth Amendment warrant requirement, but instead obtain a warrant from a magistrate u2019 144 In Stanford v. Texas,145 the Court voided a seizure of more than 2,000 books, pamphlets, and other documents pursuant to a warrant 
that merely authorized the seizure of books, pamphlets, and other written instruments concerning the Communist Party of Texas The constitutional requirement that warrants must particularly describe the things to be seized is to be accorded the 
most scrupulous exactitude when the things are books, and the basis for their seizure is the ideas which they contain No less a standard could be faithful to First Amendment freedoms 146 However, the First Amendment does not bar the issuance or execution 
of a warrant to search a newsroom to obtain photographs of demonstrators who had injured several policemen, although the Court appeared to suggest that a magistrate asked to issue such a warrant should guard against interference with press freedoms through limits on type, scope, and 
intrusiveness of the search.147 135 Marcus v. Search Warrant, 367 U.S. 717, 730 31 (1961); Stanford v. Texas, 379 U.S. 476, 485 (1965) For First Amendment implications of seizures under the Federal Racketeer In ufb02uenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (RICO), see

county in the prosecutorial district as defined in G.S nhe power and effectiveness of a closing argument is a vital part of the adversarial process that forms the basis of our justice system nA well-reasoned, well-articulated closing argument can be a critical part of 
winning a case nHowever, such argument, no matter how effective, must: (I) be devoid of counsel's personal opinion; (2) avoid name-calling and/or references to matters beyond the record; (3) be premised on logical deductions, not on appeals to passion or prejudice; 
and (4) be constructed from fair inferences drawn only from evidence properly admitted at trial nMoreover, professional decorum requires that tactics such as name-calling and showmanship must defer to a higher standard There are many situations in which a 
district attorney may deem it advisable to initiate criminal proceedings by indictment, e.g., where there is no fear of flight by a defendant, or where there exists a need for confidentiality to avoid needless harm to the reputation of a prospective accused nIn such instances, a 
prosecutor may elect to eschew the needless waste of judicial and prosecutorial resources which an unnecessary probable cause hearing would engender nSince the availability of this discretion to a district attorney bears a rational relationship to a legitimate governmental 
objective, i.e., the more efficient administration of criminal justice, it is not subject to constitutional challenge There are many situations in which a district attorney may deem it advisable to initiate criminal proceedings by indictment, e.g., where there is no fear of

entitled to have a fair trial which is conducted before an impartial judge and unprejudiced jury in an atmosphere of calm deliberation n2d 561 (1971).The obligation to take steps to assure a defendant right to a fair trial rests upon the shoulders of both the presiding 
judge and the district attorney n2d 762 (1954).However, it should be noted that the obligation of the district attorney to conduct himself in such a manner as to assure the right to a fair trial does in no way lessen his obligation to the state to prosecute criminal charges to the 
best of his abilities on the basis of the evidence that he is able to bring before the jury nAccordingly, counsel is given wide latitude in the argument of hotly contested trials, subject to the exercise of the sound discretion of the presiding judge The district attorney has 
the right and the duty to cross-examine vigorously a defendant who takes the stand in his own defense, State v. Ross, 275 N.C. 550, 169 S.E The district attorney performance of his duties as public prosecutor is tempered by his obligation to the defendant to assure that

a witness n2d 604 (1965).The test that is to be applied is whether the evidence tends to shed any light upon the subject matter of the inquiry or has as its only effect the exciting of prejudice or sympathy nPolice or Prosecutor Misconduct Is at Root of Half of Exoneration 
Cases, Study Finds Official misconduct played a role in the criminal convictions of more than half of innocent people who were later exonerated, according to a new report by a registry that tracks wrongful convictions n Official misconduct damages truth-seeking by our 
criminal justice system and undermines public confidence, Samuel Gross, a professor emeritus of law at the University of Michigan and the report lead author, said in a statement on Tuesday n The great majority of wrongful convictions are never discovered, so 
the scope of the problem is much greater than these numbers show The study, which is based on 2,400 exonerations recorded in the registry from 1989 until early 2019, found that prosecutors and police officers committed misconduct at comparable rates (30 percent and 34 
percent) nIn federal cases, however, prosecutors committed misconduct more than twice as often as police, especially in federal white-collar cases in which they committed misconduct seven times as often as police, according to the report The report 
details the different types of misconduct that can occur at different stages of a case nNearly all of the official misconduct identified falls into five general categories: witness tampering, misconduct in interrogations, fabricating evidence, concealing exculpatory evidence and 
misconduct at trial n As few police officers as there are who are arrested and convicted of official misconduct, there are almost no prosecutors who face such consequences, Mr. Feinman said, adding, I ve never heard of any prosecutor being arrested for 
misconduct, and almost no prosecutors are fired or disbarred for misconduct Jeffrey Bellin, a professor at William & Mary Law School in Williamsburg, Va., and a former prosecutor, called the trial of an innocent person the most important test of our criminal justice 
system He said it was not surprising that when the system fails that test by convicting someone who is innocent, police and prosecutors are often implicated in that failure At the same time, this is a part of the problem that can most easily be 
fixed, Professor Bellin said n Prosecutors need to see themselves as part of a system that does justice, not as unilaterally responsible for convicting those who they perceive to be guilty Speedy trial The prosecutorial indifference and the judicial

based on race, religion, or some other impermissible classification We are not inclined to interpret our state constitution to require more.2 The Court then stated that the due process inquiry must consider the reasons for the delay as well as the prejudice to the 
accused Id nIn determining whether the defendant in the present case is entitled to dismissal of the charges against him for violation of his constitutional due process right to a speedy trial, we must first consider the reasons for the pre-indictment delay nIt is clear, 
however, that the State decision to postpone indictment until 1981 was sound nIn Lovasco, the United States Supreme Court wrote: In our view, investigative delay is fundamentally unlike delay undertaken by the Government solely to gain tactical advantage over 
the accused nIn a pre-Lovasco case, this Court recognized the legitimacy of pre-indictment investigative delay, stating that the legitimate need to protect the existence of an ongoing undercover investigation from exposure has been frequently recognized by the 
federal courts as a reasonable justification for delay in bringing an indictment State v. Dietz, 289 N.C. 488, 492, 223 S.E nComment: The responsibility of a public prosecutor, which for these purposes includes a government lawyer having a prosecutorial role, differs from 
that of the usual advocate; his duty is to seek justice, not merely to convict This special duty exists because (1) the prosecutor represents the sovereign and therefore should use restraint in the discretionary exercise of government powers, such as in the selection of 
cases to prosecute; (2) during trial the prosecutor is not only an advocate but he also may make decisions normally made by an individual client, and those affecting the public interest should be fair to all; and (3) in our system of criminal justice the accused is to be 
given the benefit of all reasonable doubts With respect to evidence and witnesses, the prosecutor has responsibilities different from those of a lawyer in private practice; the prosecutor should make timely disclosure to the defense of available evidence, known to him, that tends 
to negate the guilt of the accused, mitigate the degree of the offense, or reduce the punishment nFurther, a prosecutor should not intentionally avoid pursuit of evidence merely because he believes it will damage the prosecutor case or aid the accused The exception in 
paragraph (D) recognizes that a prosecutor may seek an appropriate protective order from the tribunal if disclosure of information to the defense could result in substantial harm to an individual or to the public interest nRULE 7.2 Representing the Client Within the Bounds of the 
Law (A) In his representation of a client, a lawyer shall not: (1) File a suit, assert a position, conduct a defense, controvert an issue, delay a trial, or take other action on behalf of his client when he knows or when it is obvious that such action would be frivolous 
or would serve merely to harass or maliciously injure another n(2) Knowingly advance a claim or defense that is unwarranted under existing law, except that he may advance such claim or defense if it can be supported by good faith argument for an extension, modification, or 
reversal of existing law nA lawyer for the defendant in a criminal proceeding, or the respondent in a proceeding that could result in incarceration, may nevertheless so defend as to require that every element of the case against his client be established n(3) Conceal or 
knowingly fail to disclose that which he is required by law to reveal n(4)) Knowingly make a false statement of law or fact n(5) Knowingly use perjured testimony or false evidence n(7) Unlawfully obstruct another party access to evidence or unlawfully alter, destroy or 
conceal a document or other material having potential evidentiary value n(8) Counsel or assist his client in conduct that the lawyer knows to be illegal or fraudulent n(9) Knowingly engage in other illegal conduct or conduct contrary to a Disciplinary Rule n(B) A lawyer who 
receives information clearly establishing that: (1) His client intends to or has, in the course of the representation, perpetrated a fraud upon a person or tribunal shall promptly call upon his client to rectify the same, and, if the client refuses or is unable to do so, he 
shall discontinue his representation of the client in that matter; and if the representation involves litigation, the lawyer shall (if applicable rules require) request the tribunal to permit him to withdraw, but without necessarily revealing his reason for wishing to 
withdraw n(2) A person other than his client has perpetrated a fraud upon a tribunal shall promptly reveal the fraud to the tribunal The advocate task is to present the client case with persuasive force nPerformance of that duty while maintaining confidences 
of the client is qualified by the advocate duty of candor to the tribunal nHowever, an advocate does not vouch for the evidence submitted in a cause; the tribunal is responsible for assessing its probative value nAn advocate is responsible for pleadings and other 
documents prepared for litigation, but is usually not required to have personal knowledge of matters asserted therein, for litigation documents ordinarily present assertions by the client, or by someone on the client behalf, and not assertions by the lawyer nHowever, an 
assertion purporting to be of the lawyer own knowledge, as in an affidavit by the lawyer or in a statement in open court, may properly be made only when the lawyer knows the assertion is true or believes it to be true on the basis of a reasonably diligent inquiry The 
obligation prescribed in Rule 7.1(A)(4)not to counsel a client to commit or assist the client in committing a fraud applies in litigation nIf the false evidence is introduced before the lawyer discovers its falsity, the lawyer shall reveal the fraud to the tribunal When false 
evidence is offered by the client, however, a conflict may arise between the lawyer duty to keep the client revelations confidential and the duty of candor to the court nIf the persuasion is ineffective, the lawyer must seek to withdraw The prosecutor in a 
criminal or quasi-criminal case shall: (A) Refrain from prosecuting a charge that he knows is not supported by probable cause, unless otherwise directed by statutory mandate; (B) Make reasonable efforts to assure that the accused has been advised of the right to, and the 
procedure for obtaining, counsel and has been given reasonable opportunity to obtain counsel; (C) Not seek to obtain from an unrepresented accused a waiver of important pretrial rights, such as the right to a preliminary hearing; (D) Make timely disclosure to the defense 
of all evidence or information known to him that tends to negate the guilt of the accused or mitigates the offense, and, in connection with sentencing, disclose to the defense and to the tribunal all unprivileged mitigating information known to him, except when he is relieved of 
this responsibility by a protective order of the tribunal; and (E) Exercise reasonable care to prevent investigators, law enforcement personnel, employees, or other persons assisting or associated with him from making an extrajudicial statement that he would be prohibited from
