trial nProsecutorial Misconduct Cases And Examples June 4, 2020 By Samuel Partida, Jr. What is prosecutorial misconduct? If you ask most criminal defendants they d tell you if you want to see an an example of prosecutorial misconduct all you have to do is look at his 
case The law, in fact, gives prosecutors wide latitude to operate and it takes pretty obvious and egregious conduct before a court will finds that prosecutorial misconduct has occurred nProsecutorial Misconduct A prejudicial denial of due process can occur where an indictment 
is procured through prosecutorial misconduct n The due process rights of a defendant may be violated if the prosecutor deliberately or intentionally misleads the grand jury, uses known perjured or false testimony, or presents other deceptive or inaccurate evidence 
Oliver, 368 Ill. App Therefore, prosecutorial misconduct is a general description of wrong doing by a prosecutorial, but a defendant has to point to some prejudice before he can ask for a dismissal or a suppression of evidence To warrant dismissal of the indictment, the denial 
of due process must be unequivocally clear, and the prejudice must be actual and substantial nProsecutorial misconduct resulting in a due process violation is actually and substantially prejudicial only if the grand jury would not have otherwise indicted the defendant nIn a 
grand jury room prejudice is shown if the evidence was so weak that the misconduct induced the grand jury to indict n3d at 697-98. n law, sua sponte (Latin: "of his, her, its or their own accord ") or suo motu ( "on its own motion ") describes an act of authority taken 
without formal prompting from another party The term is usually applied to actions by a judge taken without a prior motion or request from the parties nsuborned perjury 1We acknowledge the principle discussed in the special concurrence credibility determinations are to be 
made by the trier of fact nAgain, because we are at first stage, we accept the affidavit as true nIn reviewing a postconviction petition, it is the role of the trial court to act as the trier of fact, making credibility determinations, deciding the weight to give testimony and 
evidence, and resolving evidentiary conflicts nHammond affidavit is sufficient to survive first stage due to the fact it contains an allegation that he was under instruction, inferentially by a prosecuting entity, to omit certain highly relevant facts nat the 
second stage if the judge ruling on the postconviction petition presided over the defendant trial, or at the third stage if someone other than the trial judge rules on the petition n3d at 40.25While I agree with the majority that defendant 
postconviction petition is sufficient to survive the first stage of postconviction proceedings, I disagree with the majority characterization of Hammond testimony as unreliable The reliability or unreliability of Hammond testimony 
is to be determined by the trial court, acting as the trier of fact, at the second or third stage of postconviction proceedings nFailing to correct trial testimony known to be perjured by a government witness nFinally, the analysis in the investigation report 
should include a fair observation of the investigator performance how he or she handled and analyzed evidence and intelligence and consider how that performance affected, both positively and negatively, the investigation conclusions nAs an investigator, 
however, it can lead to disastrous results nIn simple terms, confirmation bias occurs when you develop a hypothesis and then view the evidence in a light that helps confirm that hypothesis The job of an investigator, however, is to gather and evaluate all evidence from which an 
objective conclusion can be reached Witness tampering: When it comes to most criminal investigations, police officers owe a duty to the public at large, not to any particular individuals nIf police do investigate, they still have no duty to follow up on every lead or 
expend every resource in a criminal investigation nBecause no duty of care is owed to a particular victim, victims generally don't have any legal recourse in court, even when police were negligent in their investigation or in failing to investigate (absent extraordinary 
circumstances discussed below) nFile a Complaint With Internal Affairs, Police Disciplinary Board, or a Governing Body An aggrieved victim or witness who believes police engaged in misconduct or were negligent in their investigation may file a complaint with one or more of the 
following: the internal affairs department for the law enforcement agency a state or local police disciplinary board a state or local citizen oversight board, or the U.S. Department of Justice or a state human rights department (to report civil rights violations) nA victim could 
also file a complaint with the local or state governing body that oversees and funds the law enforcement agency nFor instance, a person could raise issues relating to a police department with the city council nRemedies Available Through Complaints on Police Officers The 
available remedies will usually depend on the authority granted to these agencies or departments nIt's possible a complaint could start the wheels of justice and push the police to conduct or reopen an investigation The complaint might also lead to an investigation of the 
police officer's conduct or department policies and procedures nIf an oversight board or agency determines an officer was negligent or engaged in misconduct or unethical behavior, it might issue one or more of the following disciplinary actions: oral or written remand suspension 
with or without pay demotion mandatory training decertification (take away their officer licensing or certification), or termination nIn egregious cases, an officer could be charged with a criminal offense, such as misconduct of a public officer, harassment, illegally concealing 
or tampering with evidence, or making threats nCivil Rights Violations and Discrimination If an officer's failure to investigate a crime takes the form of discrimination, a person may have a civil rights action against the officer or the department nFor instance, if police 
routinely fail to investigate domestic violence reports made by Black women, a victim might be able to prove this inaction amounts to racial and gender discrimination a violation of equal protection under the law nAnother example would be the failure of police to investigate 
suspected murders of missing Indigenous women nIn these types of cases, a person could file a federal or state civil rights action These lawsuits can be difficult to win but monetary damages are possible if successful nIn cases where a police agency, as a whole, engages in 
discriminatory investigative practice, the attorney general can sue the agency and ask the court to order the agency to change its practices (usually through a plan overseen by a court and the Justice Department) nPolice may also be subject to criminal prosecution and penalties 
for depriving a person of their constitutional rights nCAUSES OF POLICE OFFICER INADEQUACY IN INVESTIGATIONS The term "failure to investigate properly " covers several issues related to the police force's inadequate handling of a case This can include any of the following: 
Failure to secure a crime scene nFailure to maintain the appropriate chain of custody The unlawful searching of you or your property nFailure to serve a suspect their Miranda rights upon arrest The use of excessive force nSexual assault or sexual coercion by a law 
enforcement officer nOther certain circumstances where your rights are violated nA situation where you were discriminated against by police nOne of our country's basic constitutional rights is that a person cannot be arrested and charged with a crime without being served 
Miranda warnings nMiranda rights are when, upon a suspect's arrest, the arresting officer explains to the suspect that he or she has the fifth Amendment right not to make incriminating statements about themself The Miranda rights that must be given upon a suspect's arrest are: 
The right to remain silent The warning that any statement they make can be used against them in a court of law The right to an attorneyand this right includes a court-appointed public defender if they cannot afford to secure their own private legal counsel nIn addition to 
Miranda warnings, the police must adhere to ethical guidelines like having probable cause when searching a suspect or their property as well as either consent to search or a search warrant that gives them the legal right to search nFor example, if you are charged with the intent 
to sell drugs, but the search of your vehicle where they found the drugs was searched without probable cause, consent, or a search warrantthen you may have a case against the arresting officer nFailing To Maintain The Chain Of Custody When evidence is collected at a crime scene, 
it's vital that the police officers at the scene follow the proper chain of custody for evidence What this means is that there are specific guidelines on how to handle, document, and store evidence from the scene of a crime nIf throughout a criminal investigation, a police 
officer fails to maintain the pre-established chain of custody, they have failed to properly investigate the crime nExamples of errors in maintaining the chain of custody are: Not securing evidence properly, for instance not tagging or bagging it nNot restricting who handles or 
sees the evidence When there is a failure to maintain the chain of custody, a judge may rule that the evidence cannot be brought before the court This means that valuable evidence that could secure a conviction would not be allowed to be referenced in court due to the mistake 
of the officer nFailing To Properly Collect Evidence Failing to collect evidence from the scene that may pertain to the investigation can make a huge impact on any investigation The type of evidence that can be collected at a crime scene are: Fingerprints Footprints Hair Nails 
Blood Other cell tissue from a victim or perpetrator Weapons Residue from guns Fire accelerants Drugs or alcohol If any evidence is missed or if the evidence is not handled with care, it can lead to the arrest of the wrong person or result in no arrest at all for a crime committed 
against you nFailing To Secure The Crime Scene After police are called to the scene of a crime, it is the responsibility of the very first officer on the scene to secure the crime scene What this means is that as soon as law enforcement arrives at the scene, they must establish 
a marked barrier to limit anyone other than investigators from interacting with the crime scene nIf anyone tampers with evidence or there is DNA left behind from individuals present at the crime scene due to the first officer on the scene failing to secure the scene, it can lead 
to life-changing false imprisonments or the failure to collect evidence that might have led to a suspect nFrom a fingerprint accidentally getting smudged, a shoe print getting walked over, or someone not connected to the crime at all walking through and having a loose hair fall at 
the scene, any time there are facts that impact the crime scene that could have otherwise been avoided by securing the scene, it's based on the failure of police officers nAccording to the Center for Prosecutor Integrity, studies over the past 50 years, including some of those 
cited above, indicate that in 3,625 identified cases of prosecutorial misconduct, public sanctions were imposed in only 63 cases less than 2% of the time And of that discipline, just 14 prosecutors were suspended or disbarred The Center maintains a national 
registry of prosecutorial misconduct, available at prosecutorintegrity.org This was presetned for inclusion soley to bias the judge against the defendant in order to disrupt the normal sitting or use of tabula rasa so the judge would where judges intentionally set 
aside preconceptions and biases, creating a mental blank slate to fairly evaluate legal issues nHowever, it is essential to recognize that taking this perspective to an extreme can lead to inaccurate and unfair decisions1 nEnsureing it is added to the "blank 
slate " that is employed by judges as they assume the repsponsiblities of hearing a case The concept of "tabula rasa, " or blank slate, is crucial for judges who must approach each case without preconceptions to ensure a fair trial nHowever, the scenario you describe, 
where a prosecutor attempts to influence a judge with inadmissible evidence, is an ethical violation and undermines this principle This practice can be particularly problematic as it capitalizes on cognitive biases that even experienced judges might find hard to completely 
disregard once exposed nSeveral professional opinions and studies have addressed this issue: Legal Framework and Ethical Considerations: Ethically, attorneys are bound by the duty of candor to the tribunal, as outlined in rules like the Model Rules of Professional Conduct Rule 
3.3, which obligates a lawyer not to offer evidence that they know to be false and generally requires rectifying any deception to the court nJudges are similarly bound by judicial codes of conduct that require them to remain impartial and disregard inadmissible 
evidence nEmpirical Studies on Judicial Decision-Making: There have been studies suggesting that despite their training and ethical guidelines, judges are not immune to being influenced by inadmissible evidence nFor instance, a study might explore "anchoring effects, " 
where initial impressions (such as those set by inadmissible evidence) disproportionately influence the final decision, even if such evidence is formally disregarded nLegal Doctrines and Remedies: Legal doctrines such as the "doctrine of curative admissibility " and the use 
of "limiting instructions " are employed to mitigate the effects of improperly admitted evidence nResearch and case law may explore the effectiveness of these tools in actual judicial practice nCase Studies and Legal Precedents: Reviewing historical cases where misconduct 
involving the presentation of inadmissible evidence was later uncovered can provide insights into both the short-term and long-term impacts of such actions on the justice process nFor instance, the infamous "pretrial publicity " cases often explore how external information 
(though not directly presented in court by prosecutors) influences jurors' and potentially judges' perceptions nSimilarly, misconduct by prosecutors, such as in the cases explored in "Prosecutorial Misconduct " by Joseph F. Lawless, often leads to discussions on how deeply 
such actions can undermine the integrity of the judicial process nIn constructing an argument against such unethical practices, one might employ the Toulmin Model: Claim: Introducing knowingly inadmissible evidence is unethical and undermines judicial impartiality nData: 
Empirical studies demonstrate that even judges can be influenced by initially presented inadmissible evidence due to cognitive biases Warrant: Ethical guidelines and legal standards require both judges and attorneys to disregard such evidence to maintain fairness and integrity in 
the legal process nBacking: Codes of professional conduct and judicial decisions support the exclusion of such evidence and penalize its improper introduction nRebuttal: While some may argue that judges are trained to be immune to such influences, psychological studies and 
empirical evidence suggest otherwise nConclusion: To uphold justice, strict adherence to ethical standards regarding evidence admissibility must be enforced, and any violations should be met with appropriate sanctions nSeveral states, including Texas, North Carolina, and 
Maryland, have adopted rules prohibiting improper commitment (or stake-out or precommitment) questions in juror voir dire, see, e.g., Standefer v. State, 59 S.W.3d 177, 183 (Tex n146, 162 (2007); some other federal and state jurisdictions 
have addressed the issue in the context of death-qualifying juror voir dire in capital cases, see Morgan v. Illinois, 504 U.S. 719, 735-736 (1992); U.S. v. Tsarnaev, U.S. District Court No n An improper commitment question seeks to create a bias or prejudice in 
the panelists before they have heard the evidence Rodriguez-Flores v. State, 351 S.W.3d 612, 621 (Tex This question asks the jurors whether they would resolve a person guilt based on his/her possession of a residue amount of cocaine in a crack pipe nBy 
contrast, the question, If the alleged victim is a nun, could you be fair and impartial?, is not an improper commitment question because it does not ask the panelist to resolve any issue in the case based on the fact that the victim is a nun, only to commit to what the 
law requires, i.e., being fair and impartial nNot all case-specific questions are inappropriate, of course n The proper tests for whether a question is a stake-out question are the following: (1) Does the question ask a juror to speculate or precommit to how 
that juror might vote based on any particular facts? or (2) Does it seek to discover in advance what a prospective juror decision will be under a certain state of the evidence? or (3) Does it seek to cause prospective jurors to pledge themselves to a future course of 
action and indoctrinate them regarding potential issues before the evidence has been presented and they have been instructed on the law? U. S. v. Johnson, 366 F. Supp.2d 822, 845 (N.D. Iowa 2005) nI am concerned that trial judges and attorneys are not well informed about 
or skilled at asking questions during juror voir dire nI strongly urge the courts and the bar to develop training programs on the topic of questioning prospective jurors nBy learning to keep our words out of the jurors mouths, we can achieve a more effective, trustworthy 
way of choosing a jury The act of Denial of a motion to continue is reviewed for abuse of discretion n1 Removal of a district attorney is a rare occurrence in this state; there is only one prior case where a district attorney was removed from office: In re Spivey, 
345 N.C. 404, 480 S.E.2d 693 (1997).2 Spivey held that a proceeding under 7A-66 is an inquiry; it is neither a civil suit nor a criminal prosecution Id n A proceeding resulting in the removal of an individual from public office must accord that 
individual due process of law Id n 7A-66 provides that if a hearing, with or without suspension, is ordered, the district attorney should receive immediate written notice of the proceedings and a true copy of the charges, and the matter shall be set for 
hearing not less than 10 days nor more than 30 days thereafter N.C. Gen. Stat The trial court was therefore required by statute to hold the hearing between 9 February 2012 and 29 February 2012 The trial court declined to remove or suspend the district attorney, and 
that ruling was upheld on appeal Taking into account the tight time frame for this type of proceeding prescribed by statute, and the accommodations that the trial judge made for Cline (postponing the hearing from 13 February 2012 until 20 February 2012, 
restricting the scope of the hearing to statements made by Cline, and not requiring that Cline present evidence prior to 24 February 2012) we cannot say that the trial court abused its discretion in denying Cline second motion for a continuance until March 
2012 We review the trial court admission of lay opinion testimony for abuse of discretion n, , 716 S.E.2d 255, 259 (2011) conduct giving rise to this inquiry was conduct prejudicial to the administration of justice which brings the office into disrepute, is 
supported by the evidence and the other findings The statute itself compels removal upon a finding of one of the enumerated grounds and leaves no discretion in this regard with the superior court nIt is a hearing to determine the admissibility of evidence, or the 
competency of a witness or juror nAs the subject matter of the voir dire often relates to evidence, competence or other matters that may lead to bias on behalf of the jury, the jury may be removed from the court for the voir dire nCase Analysis and Fact Gathering: Thoroughly 
review all available evidence, statements, and documents related to the case nExclusion of relevant evidence on grounds of prejudice, confusion, or waste of time n Evidence of a pertinent trait of his character offered by an accused, or by the prosecution to rebut the 
same; Rule 607. Who may impeach n If, while testifying, a witness uses a writing or object to refresh his memory, an adverse party is entitled to have the writing or object produced at the trial, hearing, or deposition in which the witness is testifying n If, 
before testifying, a witness uses a writing or object to refresh his memory for the purpose of testifying and the court in its discretion determines that the interests of justice so require, an adverse party is entitled to have those portions of any writing or of the object which 
relate to the testimony produced, if practicable, at the trial, hearing, or deposition in which the witness is testifying n A party entitled to have a writing or object produced under this rule is entitled to inspect it, to cross u2011examine the witness thereon, and to 
introduce in evidence those portions which relate to the testimony of the witness nIf production of the writing or object at the trial, hearing, or deposition is impracticable, the court may order it made available for inspection nIf it is claimed that the writing or object 
contains privileged information or information not directly related to the subject matter of the testimony, the court shall examine the writing or object in camera, excise any such portions, and order delivery of the remainder to the party entitled thereto nAny portion withheld 
over objections shall be preserved and made available to the appellate court in the event of an appeal nIf a writing or object is not produced, made available for inspection, or delivered pursuant to order under this rule, the court shall make any order justice requires, but in 
criminal cases if the prosecution elects not to comply, the order shall be one striking the testimony or, if justice so requires, declaring a mistrial nAlthough relevant, evidence may be excluded if its probative value is substantially outweighed by the danger of unfair prejudice, 
confusion of the issues, or misleading the jury, or by considerations of undue delay, waste of time, or needless presentation of cumulative evidence To prove the content of a writing, recording, or photograph, the original writing, recording, or photograph is required, except as

evidence, any objection thereto is deemed to have been waived is not simply a technical rule of procedure Were the rule otherwise, an undue if not impossible burden would be placed on the trial judge nSince the party does not object a trial judge should not have to decide

the language of State v. Black, 308 N.C. 736, 303 S.E nA party may not, after trial and judgment, comb through the transcript of the proceedings and randomly insert an exception notation in disregard of the mandates of Rule 10(b) Where no action was taken by counsel during the 
course of the proceedings, the burden is on the party alleging error to establish its right to review; that is, that an exception, by rule or law was deemed preserved or taken without any such action, or that the alleged error constitutes plain error nIn so doing, a 
party must, prior to arguing the alleged error in his brief, (a) alert the appellate court that no action was taken by counsel at the trial level, and (b) establish his right to review by asserting in what manner the exception is preserved by rule or law or, when applicable, how the 
error amounted to a plain error or defect affecting a substantial right which may be noticed although not brought to the attention of the trial court We caution that our review will be carefully limited to those errors in the exceptional case where, after reviewing the 
entire record, it can be said the claimed error is a fundamental error, something so basic, so prejudicial, so lacking in its elements that justice cannot have been done, or where the error is grave error which amounts to a denial of a fun damental right 
of the accused, or the error has resulted in a miscarriage of justice or in the denial to appellant of a fair trial or where the error is such as to seriously affect the fairness, integrity or public reputation of judicial proceedings or 
where it can be fairly said the instructional mistake had a probable impact on the jury finding that the defendant was guilty State v. Odom, 307 N.C. at 660, 300 S.E nBecause the record in the case under consideration was filed prior to Odom and

instance, jails and 911 call centers often have internal policies regarding retention of audio or video recordings, and labs often have policies regarding testing of small samples of materials that are likely to be destroyed in testing Where possible, obtain and attach to the

the subject matter of the inquiry or has as its only effect the exciting of prejudice or sympathy nPolice or Prosecutor Misconduct Is at Root of Half of Exoneration Cases, Study Finds Official misconduct played a role in the criminal convictions of more than half of 
innocent people who were later exonerated, according to a new report by a registry that tracks wrongful convictions n Official misconduct damages truthseeking by our criminal justice system and undermines public confidence, Samuel Gross, a 
professor emeritus of law at the University of Michigan and the report lead author, said in a statement on Tuesday n The great majority of wrongful convictions are never discovered, so the scope of the problem is much greater than these numbers 
show The study, which is based on 2,400 exonerations recorded in the registry from 1989 until early 2019, found that prosecutors and police officers committed misconduct at comparable rates (30 percent and 34 percent) nIn federal cases, however, prosecutors 
 committed misconduct more than twice as often as police, especially in federal whitecollar cases in which they committed misconduct seven times as often as police, according to the report The report details the different types of misconduct 
that can occur at different stages of a case nNearly all of the official misconduct identified falls into five general categories witness tampering, misconduct in interrogations, fabricating evidence, concealing exculpatory evidence and misconduct at trial n As 
few police officers as there are who are arrested and convicted of official misconduct, there are almost no prosecutors who face such consequences, Mr. Feinman said, adding, I ve never heard of any prosecutor being arrested for misconduct, and almost 
no prosecutors are fired or disbarred for misconduct Jeffrey Bellin, a professor at William & Mary Law School in Williamsburg, Va., and a former prosecutor, called the trial of an innocent person the most important test of our criminal justice 
system He said it was not surprising that when the system fails that test by convicting someone who is innocent, police and prosecutors are often implicated in that failure At the same time, this is a part of the problem that can most easily 
be fixed, Professor Bellin said n Prosecutors need to see themselves as part of a system that does justice, not as unilaterally responsible for convicting those who they perceive to be guilty Speedy trial The prosecutorial indifference and the

States Supreme Court says the federal constitution does not prohibit the use of absolute prosecutorial discretion in determining which cases to prosecute for first degree murder so long as such discretionary decisions are not based on race, religion, or some 
other impermissible classification We are not inclined to interpret our state constitution to require more.2 The Court then stated that the due process inquiry must consider the reasons for the delay as well as the prejudice to the accused Id nIn

the State decision to postpone indictment until 1981 was sound nIn Lovasco, the United States Supreme Court wrote In our view, investigative delay is fundamentally unlike delay undertaken by the Government solely to gain tactical advantage over 
the accused Id nIn a preLovasco case, this Court recognized the legitimacy of preindictment investigative delay, stating that the legitimate need to protect the existence of an ongoing undercover investigation from exposure has been 
frequently recognized by the federal courts as a reasonable justification for delay in bringing an indictment State v. Dietz, 289 N.C. 488, 492, 223 S.E nComment The responsibility of a public prosecutor, which for these purposes includes a government lawyer having 
a prosecutorial role, differs from that of the usual advocate; his duty is to seek justice, not merely to convict This special duty exists because (1) the prosecutor represents the sovereign and therefore should use restraint in the discretionary exercise of government 
powers, such as in the selection of cases to prosecute; (2) during trial the prosecutor is not only an advocate but he also may make decisions normally made by an individual client, and those affecting the public interest should be fair to all; and (3) in our system 
of criminal justice the accused is to be given the benefit of all reasonable doubts With respect to evidence and witnesses, the prosecutor has responsibilities different from those of a lawyer in private practice; the prosecutor should make timely disclosure to the 
defense of available evidence, known to him, that tends to negate the guilt of the accused, mitigate the degree of the offense, or reduce the punishment nFurther, a prosecutor should not intentionally avoid pursuit of evidence merely because he believes it will damage 
the prosecutor case or aid the accused The exception in paragraph (D) recognizes that a prosecutor may seek an appropriate protective order from the tribunal if disclosure of information to the defense could result in substantial harm to an individual or to the 
public interest nRULE 7.2 Representing the Client Within the Bounds of the Law (A) In his representation of a client, a lawyer shall not (1) File a suit, assert a position, conduct a defense, controvert an issue, delay a trial, or take other action on behalf of his client 
when he knows or when it is obvious that such action would be frivolous or would serve merely to harass or maliciously injure another n(2) Knowingly advance a claim or defense that is unwarranted under existing law, except that he may advance such claim or defense if 
it can be supported by good faith argument for an extension, modification, or reversal of existing law nA lawyer for the defendant in a criminal proceeding, or the respondent in a proceeding that could result in incarceration, may nevertheless so defend as to require 
that every element of the case against his client be established n(3) Conceal or knowingly fail to disclose that which he is required by law to reveal n(4)) Knowingly make a false statement of law or fact n(5) Knowingly use perjured testimony or false evidence n(7) 
Unlawfully obstruct another party access to evidence or unlawfully alter, destroy or conceal a document or other material having potential evidentiary value n(8) Counsel or assist his client in conduct that the lawyer knows to be illegal or fraudulent n(9) 
Knowingly engage in other illegal conduct or conduct contrary to a Disciplinary Rule n(B) A lawyer who receives information clearly establishing that (1) His client intends to or has, in the course of the representation, perpetrated a fraud upon a person or tribunal 
shall promptly call upon his client to rectify the same, and, if the client refuses or is unable to do so, he shall discontinue his representation of the client in that matter; and if the representation involves litigation, the lawyer shall (if applicable rules require) 
request the tribunal to permit him to withdraw, but without necessarily revealing his reason for wishing to withdraw n(2) A person other than his client has perpetrated a fraud upon a tribunal shall promptly reveal the fraud to the tribunal The advocate task 
is to present the client case with persuasive force nPerformance of that duty while maintaining confidences of the client is qualified by the advocate duty of candor to the tribunal nHowever, an advocate does not vouch for the evidence submitted in a 
cause; the tribunal is responsible for assessing its probative value nAn advocate is responsible for pleadings and other documents prepared for litigation, but is usually not required to have personal knowledge of matters asserted therein, for litigation 
documents ordinarily present assertions by the client, or by someone on the client behalf, and not assertions by the lawyer nHowever, an assertion purporting to be of the lawyer own knowledge, as in an affidavit by the lawyer or in a statement in open 
court, may properly be made only when the lawyer knows the assertion is true or believes it to be true on the basis of a reasonably diligent inquiry The obligation prescribed in Rule 7.1(A)(4)not to counsel a client to commit or assist the client in committing a fraud 
applies in litigation nIf the false evidence is introduced before the lawyer discovers its falsity, the lawyer shall reveal the fraud to the tribunal When false evidence is offered by the client, however, a conflict may arise between the lawyer duty to keep 
the client revelations confidential and the duty of candor to the court nIf the persuasion is ineffective, the lawyer must seek to withdraw The prosecutor in a criminal or quasicriminal case shall (A) Refrain from prosecuting a charge that he knows is not 
supported by probable cause, unless otherwise directed by statutory mandate; (B) Make reasonable efforts to assure that the accused has been advised of the right to, and the procedure for obtaining, counsel and has been given reasonable opportunity to obtain 
counsel; (C) Not seek to obtain from an unrepresented accused a waiver of important pretrial rights, such as the right to a preliminary hearing; (D) Make timely disclosure to the defense of all evidence or information known to him that tends to negate the guilt of the 
accused or mitigates the offense, and, in connection with sentencing, disclose to the defense and to the tribunal all unprivileged mitigating information known to him, except when he is relieved of this responsibility by a protective order of the tribunal; and (E) 
Exercise reasonable care to prevent investigators, law enforcement personnel, employees, or other persons assisting or associated with him from making an extrajudicial statement that he would be prohibited from making under Rule 7.7 nParticular Presumptions Where there

June 4, 2020 By Samuel Partida, Jr What is prosecutorial misconduct? If you ask most criminal defendants they d tell you if you want to see an an example of prosecutorial misconduct all you have to do is look at his case The law, in fact, gives prosecutors 
wide latitude to operate and it takes pretty obvious and egregious conduct before a court will finds that prosecutorial misconduct has occurred nProsecutorial Misconduct A prejudicial denial of due process can occur where an indictment is procured through prosecutorial 
misconduct n The due process rights of a defendant may be violated if the prosecutor deliberately or intentionally misleads the grand jury, uses known perjured or false testimony, or presents other deceptive or inaccurate evidence Oliver, 368 Ill. 
App Therefore, prosecutorial misconduct is a general description of wrong doing by a prosecutorial, but a defendant has to point to some prejudice before he can ask for a dismissal or a suppression of evidence To warrant dismissal of the indictment, the denial of 
due process must be unequivocally clear, and the prejudice must be actual and substantial nProsecutorial misconduct resulting in a due process violation is actually and substantially prejudicial only if the grand jury would not have otherwise indicted the 
defendant nIn a grand jury room prejudice is shown if the evidence was so weak that the misconduct induced the grand jury to indict n3d at 69798. n law, sua sponte (Latin "of his, her, its or their own accord ") or suo motu ( "on its own motion ") describes an 
act of authority taken without formal prompting from another party The term is usually applied to actions by a judge taken without a prior motion or request from the parties nsuborned perjury 1We acknowledge the principle discussed in the special 
concurrence credibility determinations are to be made by the trier of fact nAgain, because we are at first stage, we accept the affidavit as true nIn reviewing a postconviction petition, it is the role of the trial court to act as the trier of fact, making 
credibility determinations, deciding the weight to give testimony and evidence, and resolving evidentiary conflicts nHammond affidavit is sufficient to survive first stage due to the fact it contains an allegation that he was under instruction, inferentially by 
a prosecuting entity, to omit certain highly relevant facts nat the second stage if the judge ruling on the postconviction petition presided over the defendant trial, or at the third stage if someone other than the trial judge rules on the petition n3d 
at 40.25While I agree with the majority that defendant postconviction petition is sufficient to survive the first stage of postconviction proceedings, I disagree with the majority characterization of Hammond testimony as unreliable 
The reliability or unreliability of Hammond testimony is to be determined by the trial court, acting as the trier of fact, at the second or third stage of postconviction proceedings nFailing to correct trial testimony known to be perjured by a government 
witness nFinally, the analysis in the investigation report should include a fair observation of the investigator performance how he or she handled and analyzed evidence and intelligence and consider how that performance affected, both 
positively and negatively, the investigation conclusions nAs an investigator, however, it can lead to disastrous results nIn simple terms, confirmation bias occurs when you develop a hypothesis and then view the evidence in a light that helps 
confirm that hypothesis The job of an investigator, however, is to gather and evaluate all evidence from which an objective conclusion can be reached Witness tampering When it comes to most criminal investigations, police officers owe a duty to the public at 
large, not to any particular individuals nIf police do investigate, they still have no duty to follow up on every lead or expend every resource in a criminal investigation nBecause no duty of care is owed to a particular victim, victims generally don't have any legal 
recourse in court, even when police were negligent in their investigation or in failing to investigate (absent extraordinary circumstances discussed below) nFile a Complaint With Internal Affairs, Police Disciplinary Board, or a Governing Body An aggrieved victim or 
witness who believes police engaged in misconduct or were negligent in their investigation may file a complaint with one or more of the following the internal affairs department for the law enforcement agency a state or local police disciplinary board a state or local 
citizen oversight board, or the U.S. Department of Justice or a state human rights department (to report civil rights violations) nA victim could also file a complaint with the local or state governing body that oversees and funds the law enforcement 
agency nFor instance, a person could raise issues relating to a police department with the city council nRemedies Available Through Complaints on Police Officers The available remedies will usually depend on the authority granted to these agencies or 
departments nIt's possible a complaint could start the wheels of justice and push the police to conduct or reopen an investigation The complaint might also lead to an investigation of the police officer's conduct or department policies and procedures nIf an 
oversight board or agency determines an officer was negligent or engaged in misconduct or unethical behavior, it might issue one or more of the following disciplinary actions oral or written remand suspension with or without pay demotion mandatory training 
decertification (take away their officer licensing or certification), or termination nIn egregious cases, an officer could be charged with a criminal offense, such as misconduct of a public officer, harassment, illegally concealing or tampering with evidence, or making 
threats nCivil Rights Violations and Discrimination If an officer's failure to investigate a crime takes the form of discrimination, a person may have a civil rights action against the officer or the department nFor instance, if police routinely fail 
to investigate domestic violence reports made by Black women, a victim might be able to prove this inaction amounts to racial and gender discrimination a violation of equal protection under the law nAnother example would be the failure of police to 
investigate suspected murders of missing Indigenous women nIn these types of cases, a person could file a federal or state civil rights action These lawsuits can be difficult to win but monetary damages are possible if successful nIn cases where a police agency, 
as a whole, engages in discriminatory investigative practice, the attorney general can sue the agency and ask the court to order the agency to change its practices (usually through a plan overseen by a court and the Justice Department) nPolice may also be subject 
to criminal prosecution and penalties for depriving a person of their constitutional rights nCAUSES OF POLICE OFFICER INADEQUACY IN INVESTIGATIONS The term "failure to investigate properly " covers several issues related to the police force's inadequate handling of 
a case This can include any of the following Failure to secure a crime scene nFailure to maintain the appropriate chain of custody The unlawful searching of you or your property nFailure to serve a suspect their Miranda rights upon arrest The 
use of excessive force nSexual assault or sexual coercion by a law enforcement officer nOther certain circumstances where your rights are violated nA situation where you were discriminated against by police nOne of our country's basic constitutional rights is 
that a person cannot be arrested and charged with a crime without being served Miranda warnings nMiranda rights are when, upon a suspect's arrest, the arresting officer explains to the suspect that he or she has the fifth Amendment right not to make incriminating 
statements about themself The Miranda rights that must be given upon a suspect's arrest are The right to remain silent The warning that any statement they make can be used against them in a court of law The right to an attorneyand this right includes a 
courtappointed public defender if they cannot afford to secure their own private legal counsel nIn addition to Miranda warnings, the police must adhere to ethical guidelines like having probable cause when searching a suspect or their property as well as either consent 
to search or a search warrant that gives them the legal right to search nFor example, if you are charged with the intent to sell drugs, but the search of your vehicle where they found the drugs was searched without probable cause, consent, or a search warrantthen you 
may have a case against the arresting officer nFailing To Maintain The Chain Of Custody When evidence is collected at a crime scene, it's vital that the police officers at the scene follow the proper chain of custody for evidence What this means is that there 
are specific guidelines on how to handle, document, and store evidence from the scene of a crime nIf throughout a criminal investigation, a police officer fails to maintain the preestablished chain of custody, they have failed to properly investigate the 
crime nExamples of errors in maintaining the chain of custody are Not securing evidence properly, for instance not tagging or bagging it nNot restricting who handles or sees the evidence When there is a failure to maintain the chain of custody, a judge may 
rule that the evidence cannot be brought before the court This means that valuable evidence that could secure a conviction would not be allowed to be referenced in court due to the mistake of the officer nFailing To Properly Collect Evidence Failing to collect 
evidence from the scene that may pertain to the investigation can make a huge impact on any investigation The type of evidence that can be collected at a crime scene are Fingerprints Footprints Hair Nails Blood Other cell tissue from a victim or perpetrator Weapons 
Residue from guns Fire accelerants Drugs or alcohol If any evidence is missed or if the evidence is not handled with care, it can lead to the arrest of the wrong person or result in no arrest at all for a crime committed against you nFailing To Secure The Crime Scene 
After police are called to the scene of a crime, it is the responsibility of the very first officer on the scene to secure the crime scene What this means is that as soon as law enforcement arrives at the scene, they must establish a marked barrier to limit anyone 
other than investigators from interacting with the crime scene nIf anyone tampers with evidence or there is DNA left behind from individuals present at the crime scene due to the first officer on the scene failing to secure the scene, it can lead to lifechanging 
false imprisonments or the failure to collect evidence that might have led to a suspect nFrom a fingerprint accidentally getting smudged, a shoe print getting walked over, or someone not connected to the crime at all walking through and having a loose hair fall at the 
scene, any time there are facts that impact the crime scene that could have otherwise been avoided by securing the scene, it's based on the failure of police officers nAccording to the Center for Prosecutor Integrity, studies over the past 50 years, 
including some of those cited above, indicate that in 3,625 identified cases of prosecutorial misconduct, public sanctions were imposed in only 63 cases less than 2% of the time And of that discipline, just 14 prosecutors were suspended or 
disbarred The Center maintains a national registry of prosecutorial misconduct, available at prosecutorintegrity.org This was presetned for inclusion soley to bias the judge against the defendant in order to disrupt the normal sitting or use of tabula 
rasa so the judge would where judges intentionally set aside preconceptions and biases, creating a mental blank slate to fairly evaluate legal issues nHowever, it is essential to recognize that taking this perspective to an extreme can lead to 
inaccurate and unfair decisions1 nEnsureing it is added to the "blank slate " that is employed by judges as they assume the repsponsiblities of hearing a case The concept of "tabula rasa, " or blank slate, is crucial for judges who must approach each case 
without preconceptions to ensure a fair trial nHowever, the scenario you describe, where a prosecutor attempts to influence a judge with inadmissible evidence, is an ethical violation and undermines this principle This practice can be particularly problematic as it 
capitalizes on cognitive biases that even experienced judges might find hard to completely disregard once exposed nSeveral professional opinions and studies have addressed this issue Legal Framework and Ethical Considerations Ethically, attorneys are bound by the duty of 
candor to the tribunal, as outlined in rules like the Model Rules of Professional Conduct Rule 3.3, which obligates a lawyer not to offer evidence that they know to be false and generally requires rectifying any deception to the court nJudges are similarly bound by 
judicial codes of conduct that require them to remain impartial and disregard inadmissible evidence nEmpirical Studies on Judicial DecisionMaking There have been studies suggesting that despite their training and ethical guidelines, judges are not immune to being 
influenced by inadmissible evidence nFor instance, a study might explore "anchoring effects, " where initial impressions (such as those set by inadmissible evidence) disproportionately influence the final decision, even if such evidence is formally 
disregarded nLegal Doctrines and Remedies Legal doctrines such as the "doctrine of curative admissibility " and the use of "limiting instructions " are employed to mitigate the effects of improperly admitted evidence nResearch and case law may explore the 
effectiveness of these tools in actual judicial practice nCase Studies and Legal Precedents Reviewing historical cases where misconduct involving the presentation of inadmissible evidence was later uncovered can provide insights into both the shortterm and longterm 
impacts of such actions on the justice process nFor instance, the infamous "pretrial publicity " cases often explore how external information (though not directly presented in court by prosecutors) influences jurors' and potentially judges' 
perceptions nSimilarly, misconduct by prosecutors, such as in the cases explored in "Prosecutorial Misconduct " by Joseph F. Lawless, often leads to discussions on how deeply such actions can undermine the integrity of the judicial process nIn constructing an 
argument against such unethical practices, one might employ the Toulmin Model Claim Introducing knowingly inadmissible evidence is unethical and undermines judicial impartiality nData Empirical studies demonstrate that even judges can be influenced by initially 
presented inadmissible evidence due to cognitive biases Warrant Ethical guidelines and legal standards require both judges and attorneys to disregard such evidence to maintain fairness and integrity in the legal process nBacking Codes of professional conduct 
and judicial decisions support the exclusion of such evidence and penalize its improper introduction nRebuttal While some may argue that judges are trained to be immune to such influences, psychological studies and empirical evidence suggest otherwise nConclusion 
To uphold justice, strict adherence to ethical standards regarding evidence admissibility must be enforced, and any violations should be met with appropriate sanctions nSeveral states, including Texas, North Carolina, and Maryland, have adopted 
rules prohibiting improper commitment (or stakeout or precommitment) questions in juror voir dire, see, e.g., Standefer v. State, 59 S.W.3d 177, 183 (Tex n146, 162 (2007); some other federal and state jurisdictions have 
addressed the issue in the context of deathqualifying juror voir dire in capital cases, see Morgan v. Illinois, 504 U.S. 719, 735736 (1992); U.S. v. Tsarnaev, U.S. District Court No n An improper commitment question seeks to create a bias 
or prejudice in the panelists before they have heard the evidence RodriguezFlores v nState, 351 S.W.3d 612, 621 (Tex This question asks the jurors whether they would resolve a person guilt based on hisher possession of a residue amount of cocaine 
in a crack pipe nBy contrast, the question, If the alleged victim is a nun, could you be fair and impartial?, is not an improper commitment question because it does not ask the panelist to resolve any issue in the case based on the fact that the victim is 
a nun, only to commit to what the law requires, i.e., being fair and impartial n The proper tests for whether a question is a stakeout question are the following (1) Does the question ask a juror to speculate or precommit to how that juror might 
vote based on any particular facts? or (2) Does it seek to discover in advance what a prospective juror decision will be under a certain state of the evidence? or (3) Does it seek to cause prospective jurors to pledge themselves to a future course of action 
and indoctrinate them regarding potential issues before the evidence has been presented and they have been instructed on the law? U nS nv. Johnson, 366 F. Supp.2d 822, 845 (N.D. Iowa 2005) nI am concerned that trial judges and attorneys are not well informed 
about or skilled at asking questions during juror voir dire nI strongly urge the courts and the bar to develop training programs on the topic of questioning prospective jurors nBy learning to keep our words out of the jurors mouths, we can achieve a more 
effective, trustworthy way of choosing a jury The act of Denial of a motion to continue is reviewed for abuse of discretion n1 Removal of a district attorney is a rare occurrence in this state; there is only one prior case where a district attorney was removed

prejudicial to the administration of justice which brings the office into disrepute, is supported by the evidence and the other findings The statute itself compels removal upon a finding of one of the enumerated grounds and leaves no discretion in this regard with the 
superior court nIt is a hearing to determine the admissibility of evidence, or the competency of a witness or juror nAs the subject matter of the voir dire often relates to evidence, competence or other matters that may lead to bias on behalf of the jury, the jury 
may be removed from the court for the voir dire nCase Analysis and Fact Gathering Thoroughly review all available evidence, statements, and documents related to the case nExclusion of relevant evidence on grounds of prejudice, confusion, or waste of time n 
Evidence of a pertinent trait of his character offered by an accused, or by the prosecution to rebut the same; Rule 607 Who may impeach n If, while testifying, a witness uses a writing or object to refresh his memory, an adverse party is entitled to have the 
writing or object produced at the trial, hearing, or deposition in which the witness is testifying n If, before testifying, a witness uses a writing or object to refresh his memory for the purpose of testifying and the court in its discretion determines that 
the interests of justice so require, an adverse party is entitled to have those portions of any writing or of the object which relate to the testimony produced, if practicable, at the trial, hearing, or deposition in which the witness is testifying n A party 
entitled to have a writing or object produced under this rule is entitled to inspect it, to cross u2011examine the witness thereon, and to introduce in evidence those portions which relate to the testimony of the witness nIf production of the writing or object at the 
trial, hearing, or deposition is impracticable, the court may order it made available for inspection nIf it is claimed that the writing or object contains privileged information or information not directly related to the subject matter of the testimony, the court 
shall examine the writing or object in camera, excise any such portions, and order delivery of the remainder to the party entitled thereto nAny portion withheld over objections shall be preserved and made available to the appellate court in the event of 
an appeal nIf a writing or object is not produced, made available for inspection, or delivered pursuant to order under this rule, the court shall make any order justice requires, but in criminal cases if the prosecution elects not to comply, the order shall be one 
striking the testimony or, if justice so requires, declaring a mistrial nAlthough relevant, evidence may be excluded if its probative value is substantially outweighed by the danger of unfair prejudice, confusion of the issues, or misleading the jury, or by considerations 
of undue delay, waste of time, or needless presentation of cumulative evidence To prove the content of a writing, recording, or photograph, the original writing, recording, or photograph is required, except as otherwise provided in these rules or by

we considered the effect of our Rule 10(b)(2)when no objection or exception was made at trial to instructions to the jury, and adopted there the plain error rule n2d 804 (1983), we adopted the plain error rule with equal force with regard to 
Rule 10(b)(1)when no objection or exception was made at trial to evidence presented and admitted, stating that The rule that unless objection is made to the introduction of evidence at the time the evidence is offered, or unless there is a timely motion to strike the 
evidence, any objection thereto is deemed to have been waived is not simply a technical rule of procedure Were the rule otherwise, an undue if not impossible burden would be placed on the trial judge nSince the party does not object a trial judge should not have to 
decide on his own the soundness of a party trial strategy nReading the language of Rule 10(b)(1)that an exception may be properly preserved by objection noted or which by rule or law was deemed preserved or taken without any such 
action, together with the language of State v. Black, 308 N.C. 736, 303 S.E nA party may not, after trial and judgment, comb through the transcript of the proceedings and randomly insert an exception notation in disregard of the mandates of Rule 10(b) Where 
no action was taken by counsel during the course of the proceedings, the burden is on the party alleging error to establish its right to review; that is, that an exception, by rule or law was deemed preserved or taken without any such action, or that the 
alleged error constitutes plain error nIn so doing, a party must, prior to arguing the alleged error in his brief, (a) alert the appellate court that no action was taken by counsel at the trial level, and (b) establish his right to review by asserting in what manner 
the exception is preserved by rule or law or, when applicable, how the error amounted to a plain error or defect affecting a substantial right which may be noticed although not brought to the attention of the trial court We caution that our review will 
be carefully limited to those errors in the exceptional case where, after reviewing the entire record, it can be said the claimed error is a fundamental error, something so basic, so prejudicial, so lacking in its elements that justice cannot have 
been done, or where the error is grave error which amounts to a denial of a fun damental right of the accused, or the error has resulted in a miscarriage of justice or in the denial to appellant of a fair trial or where 
the error is such as to seriously affect the fairness, integrity or public reputation of judicial proceedings or where it can be fairly said the instructional mistake had a probable impact on the jury finding that the defendant was 
guilty State v. Odom, 307 N.C. at 660, 300 S.E nBecause the record in the case under consideration was filed prior to Odom and Black, and because the case involves sentences of death, we have elected to review all errors assigned, whether properly objected to 
at trial or alleged for the first time on this appeal They can't be introduced unlesseither I testify or someone else does nMiranda Rights Law Under Miranda v. Arizona (1966), suspects must be informed of their rights to remain silent and to an attorney when they 
are in custody and subject to interrogation nVoluntariness of DNA Collection Law The standard you cited indicates that a DNA sample should not be taken without a probable cause determination if the arrest is without a warrant nApplication If the suspect was not

such questions, any possible prejudice to the defendant is cured n "Our courts have recognized that there may be selectivity in prosecutions and that the exercise of this prosecutorial prerogative does not reach constitutional proportion unless there be a showing 
that the selection was deliberately based upon 'an unjustifiable standard such as race, religion or other arbitrary classification.' In order for a motion to preserve evidence to be effective, however, it should be filed as soon as possible nDefense counsel should

loss or destruction of evidence nPractice Tips uf0b7 A notice to preserve evidence should be tailored to a specific concern about particular evidence in the case The notice should identify the evidence that is of concern, specify the importance of

the witness by filing an affidavit and request with the clerk The affidavit filed with the clerk must (1) include a statement that the testimony of the person sought to be deposed is important to the defense; (2) provide the witness name; and (3) state that

cash n19CR051799-520 FILE: E:/Organization_Folder/02_Working_Folder/02_Analysis/06_Case Review Brief - Case Law Reference.docx Legal Framework and Procedural Analysis When a detective's familial relationship with a witness and bias in an affidavit for arrest 
are at play, the legal framework revolves around principles of due process, evidence integrity, and the credibility of law enforcement actions The foundational elements to consider include: Constitutional Protections: The U.S. Constitution, particularly the Fourth Amendment's 
protections against unreasonable searches and seizures and the Fourteenth Amendment's due process clause, provides the bedrock for examining the legality of the detective's actions and the affidavit's integrity nLegal Standards for Evidence: The admissibility of evidence in court 
is governed by standards that ensure fairness and reliability, as outlined in the Federal Rules of Evidence and comparable state rules nPrecedential Case Law: Key Supreme Court decisions establish the legal precedents that guide how courts handle issues of bias, credibility, and 
procedural errors in criminal investigations and prosecutions nLegal Concern: The potential for bias affecting the witness's testimony and the investigation's integrity nProcedural Analysis: Courts may scrutinize the evidence and testimony provided by the witness more closely,

 Right to challenge false statements in warrant affidavits n| Provides a procedural avenue for addressing bias in the affidavit for arrest nConclusion The scenario's analysis, grounded in First Principles Thinking, underscores the critical importance of ensuring procedural 
integrity and the accused's constitutional rights The detective's familial relationship with a witness and the bias in the affidavit for arrest introduce significant legal challenges that must be navigated with a deep understanding of constitutional protections, evidence 
standards, and the overarching principle of fairness in the criminal justice system nViolations and Illegal Acts: a) Brady Violations: The prosecution appears to have committed several Brady violations by failing to disclose exculpatory evidence nIn Brady v. Maryland, 373 U.S.

defined as a reasonable belief that a person has committed a crime The U.S. Supreme Court in Illinois v. Gates, 462 U.S. 213 (1983), established that probable cause is determined based on the "totality of the circumstances This requires a practical, common-sense 
decision whether, given all the circumstances, there is a fair probability that a crime has been committed and that the suspect committed it nAs stated in State v. Biber, 365 N.C. 162, 712 S.E.2d 874 (2011), "Probable cause requires only a probability or substantial chance of 
criminal activity, not an actual showing of such activity Examination of Factors Considered for Probable Cause: a) Accuser's Statement: Lauren Deal's statement was the primary basis for the arrest nHowever, several issues undermine its reliability: Inconsistencies in 
her account, particularly regarding the timeline of events Her admitted consumption of alcohol, which could affect her recollection The lack of immediate reporting, which allowed time for potential fabrication or influence from others Case law: In State v. Knoll, 322 N.C. 535, 
369 S.E.2d 558 (1988), the North Carolina Supreme Court held that an officer must consider the totality of the circumstances, including the credibility of the complainant, when determining probable cause n770, 803 S.E.2d 419 (2017), the court emphasized the importance of 
considering all available evidence, including exculpatory evidence, in determining probable cause nCase law: In Franks v. Delaware, 438 U.S. 154 (1978), the Supreme Court held that a defendant is entitled to a hearing if they can make a substantial preliminary showing that a false 
statement knowingly and intentionally, or with reckless disregard for the truth, was included in a warrant affidavit This conflict of interest taints the entire process nIn State v. Campbell, 282 N.C. 125, 191 S.E.2d 752 (1972), the North Carolina Supreme Court emphasized the 
importance of corroborating evidence in establishing probable cause n1999), the court held that an officer has a duty to conduct a reasonably thorough investigation prior to arresting a suspect, including consideration of exculpatory evidence n$antthinking$ The numerous flaws in

Strategies: Motion to Suppress Evidence: Given the lack of probable cause for Mr. Allen's arrest, a motion to suppress all evidence obtained as a result of the arrest should be filed under the "fruit of the poisonous tree " doctrine established in Wong Sun v. United States,

361 N.C. 389, 646 S.E.2d 727 (2007), the North Carolina Supreme Court held that evidence obtained as a result of an unlawful arrest must be suppressed nMotion to Dismiss Charges: File a motion to dismiss all charges based on the violation of Mr. Allen's constitutional rights and

321 N.C. 633, 365 S.E.2d 600 (1988), the North Carolina Supreme Court held that dismissal is appropriate when there has been irreparable prejudice to the defendant's preparation of his case nLegal Analysis: Probable cause requires facts and circumstances that would lead a 
reasonable person to believe a crime has been committed The standard is lower than beyond reasonable doubt but higher than mere suspicion nCase Law: Illinois v. Gates, 462 U.S. 213 (1983) established the "totality of the circumstances " test for probable cause nBeck v. 
Ohio, 379 U.S. 89 (1964) held that probable cause exists when "the facts and circumstances within the officers' knowledge and of which they had reasonably trustworthy information are sufficient in themselves to warrant a man of reasonable caution in the belief that an offense has 
been or is being committed This is an excellent question that requires careful analysis of Miranda doctrine and its application to custodial interrogations To properly assess when Miranda warnings should have been given to Ben Allen, we need to consider several key Supreme 
Court cases and their implications nFirst, let's review the foundational case: Miranda v. Arizona, 384 U.S. 436 (1966) Established that police must inform suspects of their rights before a custodial interrogation Rights include: right to remain silent, right to an attorney, 
warning that statements can be used against them The key question here is whether Ben Allen was in "custody " for Miranda purposes during this interview The test for custody is not simply whether the person is at a police station, but whether a reasonable person would feel 
free to leave nRelevant cases on custody determination: Oregon v. Mathiason, 429 U.S. 492 (1977) Voluntary arrival at police station and brief questioning did not constitute custody Court emphasized importance of coercive environment, not just location California v. 
Beheler, 463 U.S. 1121 (1983) Reinforced that Miranda only applies to custodial interrogation Voluntary interview at police station without arrest was not custodial Thompson v. Keohane, 516 U.S. 99 (1995) Outlined two-part inquiry for custody: a) Circumstances surrounding 
interrogation b) Given those circumstances, would a reasonable person have felt free to terminate the interrogation and leave Yarborough v. Alvarado, 541 U.S. 652 (2004) Custody determination should be based on objective circumstances, not subjective views of the interrogating 
officers or person being questioned Applying these principles to Ben Allen's situation: He came to the station voluntarily The conversation seems relatively casual at the start He expresses concerns about his job, suggesting he's thinking about consequences There's no 
indication he was told he couldn't leave However, the turning point may be when Detective Freeman starts asking specific questions about the night's events nAt this point, the interaction becomes more of an interrogation about potential criminal activity nBerkemer v. McCarty, 
468 U.S. 420 (1984) held that Miranda applies when a suspect is subjected to restraints comparable to those associated with formal arrest The key is whether there's a "formal arrest or restraint on freedom of movement of the degree associated with formal arrest It's 
worth noting that failure to give Miranda warnings doesn't necessarily make the arrest itself unlawful, but it could result in suppression of any incriminating statements made during the interrogation nUnited States v. Patane, 542 U.S. 630 (2004) held that physical evidence 
obtained as a result of unwarned statements is still admissible, absent actual coercion The exact point is somewhat subjective, but err on the side of caution to protect the admissibility of any statements made This is an important question that requires careful consideration 
of legal ethics, professional standards for law enforcement, and the broader implications for justice and due process nLet's analyze the detective's behavior through multiple lenses: Legal and Procedural Ethics: Miranda Warnings: As discussed previously, the detective

Carolina law and general jurisprudence nNorth Carolina Law Mandating Unanimous Jury Verdicts The legal framework of North Carolina robustly protects the right to a unanimous jury verdict in criminal proceedings nSection 24 of Article I of the Constitution of North Carolina 
underpins this right, ensuring that no individual can be convicted of a crime without the unanimous consent of a jury of their peers nGeneral Statute 15A-1201(a) explicitly mandates that, absent a waiver by the defendant pursuant to 15A-1201(b), a defendant in a 
criminal trial possesses the right to be tried by a jury of twelve, and that the verdict rendered by this jury must be unanimous nFurther procedural safeguards are detailed in General Statute 15A-1237, which prescribes the essential elements of a valid criminal 
verdict This statute dictates that all criminal verdicts must adhere to the following criteria: Written Form: The verdict must be documented in writing nForeperson Signature: The written verdict must be signed by the jury foreperson nUnanimity: The verdict 
must reflect the unanimous agreement of all jurors nOpen Court Return: The verdict must be formally returned by the jury in open court nRecord Inclusion: The verdict must be officially incorporated into the court record The legislative intent behind the written 
verdict requirement, as elucidated in the Official Commentary to G.S n15A-1237 and State v. Goodman, 298 N.C. 1 (1979), is to mitigate potential inaccuracies and omissions that could occur during the oral pronouncement of a verdict by the foreperson nHowever, North Carolina 
jurisprudence has also recognized that strict adherence to procedural formalities should not supersede the precise substance of a jury's decision n685 (2004), verdicts have been upheld despite minor procedural oversights, indicating a pragmatic approach where the core elements of 
a valid verdict are demonstrably present nDe Facto Acquittal Established by Jury Communication in the Allen Case The official court transcript of the Allen case proceedings contains a critical exchange that firmly supports the argument for a de facto acquittal on the

record nFundamental Principles of Double Jeopardy The principle of double jeopardy, deeply rooted in Anglo-American jurisprudence, dictates that an individual shall not be twice placed in jeopardy for the same offense This protection is designed to prevent the state, with its 
vast resources, from repeatedly attempting to convict an individual for an alleged crime The fundamental tenet of double jeopardy is that a verdict of acquittal, once rendered, is final and cannot be overturned or ignored To subject Mr. Allen to a retrial on the charge of 
first-degree forcible rape, after the jury in his initial trial unequivocally communicated their unanimous "not guilty " decision to the court and had it officially recorded, would directly contravene these foundational principles nSuch an action would undermine the finality

evidence rather than suspicion alone nConstitutional Context: Under the Fourth Amendment, citizens are protected from unlawful arrests, which include arrests made without a reasonable basis nProbable cause forms the threshold that must be met before depriving an individual of 
liberty, serving as a check on law enforcement power to ensure that any interference with personal freedom is legally justified nProbable Cause Documentation as a Procedural Safeguard Documentation as Evidence of Legitimacy: Documenting probable cause is crucial in establishing 
the legitimacy of an arrest nIt provides a written record for the court, the suspect, and the public to understand the factual basis of the detention This documentation is essential because it allows for judicial review, where a judge or magistrate can evaluate the lawfulness 
of the arrest Written Record and Chain of Justification: Probable cause documentation is typically written in an affidavit or report form and is presented to a magistrate or judge when requesting an arrest warrant This ensures a chain of justification, showing a

Cause: Probable cause is a reasonable basis for believing that a person has committed a crime, established through facts and evidence rather than speculation or personal judgment nProbable cause acts as a safeguard against arbitrary detention, ensuring that arrests are based on 
verifiable information and protecting citizens Fourth Amendment rights nLegal Importance of Probable Cause: The U.S. Constitution Fourth Amendment establishes that arrests should only be conducted when probable cause exists, either through an officer direct 
observations, reliable witness accounts, or other corroborative evidence nFor an arrest to be lawful, the evidence supporting probable cause must be substantive enough to withstand judicial review, typically documented in detail within an affidavit Typically, officers create a 
written record or affidavit outlining the basis for probable cause, especially in cases where the arrest is made without a warrant This document serves as a transparent and verifiable record for judicial review nLegal Standards and Precedents on Probable Cause 
Documentation Case Law Supporting Written Documentation: Gerstein v. Pugh (1975): This case emphasized that the Constitution requires a prompt judicial determination of probable cause for any significant detention Without written documentation, the court lacks a reliable 
basis for validating the lawfulness of detention nIllinois v. Gates (1983): This case addressed the totality of the circumstances in establishing probable cause, but it also emphasized the importance of having a documented record that a judge can review, ensuring 
that the arrest was based on objective facts rather than subjective judgment nIf there is no verifiable record of probable cause, the arrest may lack the constitutionally required basis, which could lead to significant procedural challenges nOfficers in his position are not only 
expected to know these requirements but are often responsible for teaching them, making his omission more notable nAdherence to Probable Cause Standards: Law enforcement officers are trained to document probable cause, especially in cases where the arrest occurs without a 
warrant nImplications of Probable Cause Issues on Allen Arrest Risk of Unlawful Detention: Without documented probable cause, Allen detention could be seen as lacking a legal foundation nIf the arrest cannot be shown to have met the probable cause threshold, 
the detention could be deemed unlawful, infringing upon Allen Fourth Amendment rights This would make all subsequent evidence potentially vulnerable to suppression nFruit of the Poisonous Tree Doctrine: If Allen detention is found unlawful due to insufficient 
probable cause documentation, the defense may invoke the fruit of the poisonous tree doctrine This doctrine holds that any evidence obtained from an illegal detention is tainted and should be excluded from the trial nIn this case, the absence of documented 
probable cause weakens the arrest and could endanger evidence collected thereafter nDefense Challenge Based on Constitutional Grounds: Allen defense could argue that his detention violated constitutional protections due to the lack of written probable cause nHow the 
Retrospective Order Affects Probable Cause The Magistrate Retrospective Justification: The magistrate order indicated that Allen was arrested without a warrant and that the detention was justified by probable cause This order retrospectively 
legitimizes the detention but does not provide the same legal foundation as a pre-detention warrant based on documented probable cause nProcedural and Legal Limitations of Retrospective Orders: Retrospective approval can only serve as a stopgap and does not replace the necessity 
of documented probable cause nBy approving the arrest after it had occurred without a formal written record, the magistrate order could still be subject to challenge, especially if the court finds that it circumvented standard judicial review protocols nAn 
officer credibility is central to the prosecution case, and any appearance of procedural shortcuts or omissions can erode confidence in the investigation integrity This document would undergo judicial review before or immediately after custody, ensuring 
transparency and legal compliance nFor an experienced officer, this is an unusual and legally vulnerable approach, suggesting either an intentional bypass of protocol or an assumption that his judgment would suffice nDefense Strategy and Possible Legal Challenges Motion to 
Suppress Evidence: The defense could file a motion to suppress evidence obtained post-detention, arguing that the lack of documented probable cause invalidates the arrest nIf the court agrees, key evidence collected following Allen detention could be excluded, weakening 
the prosecution case nChallenge to the Retrospective Approval Validity: By emphasizing the lack of pre-detention documentation, the defense may argue that the magistrate retrospective approval fails to meet constitutional standards, citing cases like 
Gerstein and McLaughlin that require prompt judicial review based on documented evidence The magistrate retrospective approval does not fully replace the need for pre-detention documentation, as it bypasses the usual review processes and lacks the rigor of a documented 
affidavit nIf you have further questions or would like to explore additional aspects of this case, feel free to let me know This could undermine the prosecution argument that the physical evidence conclusively supports their case nIf Allen detention occurred
